On the Bloodletting of Veins.
The veins to be bled, which course through the body, are thirty.
Of these, in the head there are sixteen: two pulsating veins, which are behind the ears, called the Two Herbs; and two arteries, which appear in the two temples; and two veins that are in the ducts of the two eyes, which are called Aspicientes; and the vein prepared in the middle of the forehead; and the vein that is at the end of the nose. And two Guidi veins, which are in the neck. And two veins that are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins that are in the lower lip. And these are the veins called Algecherich. And two veins that are under the tongue.
The veins to be bled in the arms, are five in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic, and it is on the side of the wood: and the people call it the vein of the head. And Fusca. And it is the middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilica, and a branch of the Cephalic; and the common people call it the body's vein. And Basilica, and it is placed in the domestic side; and it is also called Titillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the abdomen. And the cord of the arm, and it is placed on the elbow; and that is the one from which bloodletting is performed, and that is the one which appears above the thumb by a clear manifestation.
And Saluatella: it is the vein that is between the annular and cephalic; and it has two branches.
And in the arm and in the leg there are three veins. One of them is that which is below the thumb's curvature from the side of the wood. And the second one is the Saphena: and its place is at the heel from the domestic side. And the vein of Naessus: and its place is at the heel from the wild side.
And on the other arm there are three veins similar to those.
Chapter on bloodletting of two veins after the ears.
The bloodletting of two veins that are after the ears helps with old catarrhs, and hemigraine, and sahafati, and old ulcers of the head.
And the quality of bloodletting it is according to what I have said. And it is that the head of the sick person should be shaved; and the nape of his head should be rubbed at the place of two veins with a rough cloth rubbing well. Then the sick person should bind his neck with a bandage until the two veins appear. And their places are behind the ears, in two depressed places of the head. Translate: Therefore, examine them (the lesions) with your finger, and where you feel the pulsation of them under your finger, there mark it with the cautery.
Translate: Then take a lancet for bloodletting, and this is called Incisor (Incising). Translate: Then insert it (the lancet) under the vein into the flesh, until the bloodletting reaches the bone. Translate: Then move your hand with the vein and skin upward, and cut the vein with an incising instrument (cut the vein along with the skin). Translate: And let the length of the incision be about the size of two fingers joined together, or roughly that much, and let as much blood flow out as you wish.
Translate: Then bind it (the wound) with threads and leave it, until it heals.
Translate: And now the incision of these (veins) and their cauterization has been discussed at the beginning of this book.
Translate: However, the bloodletting of the two arteries that are in the temples is helpful for old migraine: and for headaches (praua) and constant pain (obtalmia), and for the course of effused acute excesses towards the eyes.
Translate: And the quality of bloodletting them (the arteries) is as I tell you. Translate: The sick person should bind his neck tightly until two veins appear to the sensation by their obvious appearance and their pulse is felt under your finger. Translate: Then mark them (the veins) with cautionery.
Translate: Then move the skin over the vein upward with your finger and indicate, and insert the lancet incising from below, and move the vein upward. Translate: And cut it (the vein) as you did with the other two veins: and let as much blood flow out as necessary for your need.
Then unbind the constriction of the sick person, and place your finger on the hour one [vein]. Then place [your fingers] over it and let the leeches bleed [the wound], and tie it tightly above with a strong binding; and release it when healed.
And now the recollection of these [wounds], their incision, and their removal has ended at the beginning of the book.
However, the remedy for phlebotomy of the forehead vein is after the phlebotomy of the head (Cephalic), for old facial ailments: and for migraines, redness, and foul ulcers.
And the quality of this phlebotomy is as I have told you. The sick person binds his neck with a bandage until the vein appears. Then take the tool called Fossorium (probe).
Place the sharp point that is on top of the Fossorii probe onto the vein itself and strike it with a mallet or some other tool in the same manner: and let the blood flow according to the amount you wish. Then dissolve the wound of the patient and bind it until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide leech. However, it is not necessary that the leech be of sharp point, as are the other leeches, but rather wide-mouthed. And the leech should be applied across the wound, because the bone is nearby and perhaps the leech will break when it is sharp.
The remedy for the two veins that are in the two tear ducts of both eyes is for eye itch and redness, and to relieve pain in the eye and facial discomfort.
And the quality of their leeching is that the patient should bind his own neck with a plaster. Then apply the leeches to them: and you should be standing over his head.
And the leeching should be done across, slightly shorter than the width of a small leech, and somewhat wider than a wide leech, because there is not much flesh at that spot. If the leech is too sharp, it may break.
Then let the blood flow as much as you require. Place them on top of it and bind them overnight, then unbind them.
The veins of the nose are useful for treating acute and violent fever, and facial ailments such as migraines and redness that occur on the face, particularly when it is old.
The nature of this phlebotomy is that it tightens the sick man's neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a fine, long phlebotomy lancet; and fix it in the middle of the leprosy itself, between that which separates the nose according to its correct alignment. Because the vein is not visible there, and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you hold the end of your hand with the phlebotomy lancet a little bit; and make the blood flow according to your need. Then bind it at night. For it heals quickly.
There are two benefits of phlebotomy according to Guidegium: for the constriction of shortness of breath, and the beginning of leprosy, and melancholic diseases, and impetigo and bad ulcers, and those that are corroding (herpes or eumenies).
And the nature of this phlebotomy is that it tightens the sick man under both his necks with a bandage; and the worker stands over the head of the sick man, and the sick man sits on a chair.
Then the phlebotomy vein is made slightly larger than usual for phlebotomy. Then draw out a measured amount of blood, or according to what you see according to the need that is there. Do the same with another vein.
Then release the bandage, and tighten two veins with a moderate pressure; so that the sick man is not choked. And leave it until morning. The wound heals.
Chapter on the Phlebotomy of Veins (Algebrichium). The veins according to Algebrich benefit phlebotomy because they contribute to alcohol in the mouth, and corruption of the gums; and bad ulcers; and cracks in the lips, and bad ulcers, even those that are in the nose, and around it.
And the quality of bloodletting for them is that you make the sick person sit before you, and tighten his neck with a bandage. Then roll back his lip, and look at the two veins: one is on the right side of the lip, and the second is on the left: the identification of each is through their blackness. And this is true because around them are small veins, dark ones. Make therefore an incision on each with a cutting incision.
If there is doubt about you and you do not know which one it is: then aim for a larger incision of them, and that one will be more visible.
And similarly do with the two veins that are on the upper lip: and most commonly is the custom with this: and cut the two veins that are in the lower lip.
Chapter on bloodletting: Vein under tongue after cephalic bloodletting. But of the two veins that are under the tongue, bloodletting helps: because cephalic bloodletting is for afflictions in the throat, and illnesses of the neck, and illnesses of the mouth.
And the quality of bloodletting them is that the sick person sits between your hands before the sun: and you open his mouth wide, and look under the tongue on one side, and on the other side a vein: and the color of each inclines towards blackness.
Bloodletting therefore both (veins): and be careful not to destroy your hand while bloodletting them. Because under his are the arteries, and perhaps there will be a flow of blood from these arteries.
But the three veins that are bloodletted on the arm (cubitus) have been the custom of bloodletting in all human beings.
And phlebotomy (of these) is done according to two modes. Either by pricking with a broad leech (myrtinus), or by cupping to thinness (oliuari), or by cutting with a surgical knife (cultellus), and this is called Alnsil. And this is its form.
And from these (are) the wide and the thin forms according to the size and narrowness of the veins. The form of leeching (phlebotomy with a leech) is wide, as you see. And it (Alnsil) meets for opening hollow, full, projecting, appearing, thick veins, and those which contain thick, turbid blood.
And this leech (phlebotomus) is of smaller width and more subtle in thinning.
And it (Alnsil) meets for leeching (phlebotomy) of fine veins which contain subtle yellowish blood.
But the basilica which is one of these three veins' leeching (phlebotomy) is beneficial. Because it draws blood from the ailments which are under the throat and neck, following the chest, and the stomach; and it is beneficial for leeching (phlebotomy) to the phlebotomist with the phlebotome, so that he may beware of it and be above fear, because below it is an artery. If therefore the leech (phlebotomus) misses and adds in the prick of the phlebotome, it will cut that artery and there will be a flow of blood. Therefore, the leech (phlebotomus) should not prick it with the phlebotome; rather, it should cut it with Alnsil.
If the basilica does not appear with a manifest appearance, then it is necessary to search for another one, or exchange it for another, or seek a branch of it; or use a cord of the arm in place of (the phlebotome). The thing itself is obvious; cut it open with Phlebotomus Anesil, as we have said.
If you wish for the phlebotomy of this same thing, then it is necessary before the constriction of the arm, that you touch the place until you recognize the place of the pulse. Then mark the place with pitch, afterwards arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision with Phlebotomus Alnesil, as we have said. And beware that the blow does not fall into the space from the place of the artery.
And when you see, as you tie the bandage, swelling at the place which you marked with pitch, then that swelling is swelling of the artery; avoid it therefore.
If you see blood at the phlebotomy rising, like urine in a boy, and it is subtle, red: then you shall know that it is from the blood of the artery. Therefore, approach, and put your finger on it at a long space, afterwards withdraw your finger. If blood is cut (for it is often cut), then tie the arm, and let it be; and let the weak avoid its movement; and beware of its dread.
And do not move it for a few days until it heals.
If blood is not cut and binds you: and if medicine is not present at your moment, then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For its ends contract and blood is cut off.
Or take the bark of a fig tree, and boil it; take one half-part, and tie it on the spot where the vein is constricted with a bandage and powder until another day.
Then if blood is cut off, it is good. If not (the case): then care for the same person along with him, remembering the place of the dust causing bleeding flow from the previous one. And the removal of his blood is not difficult in many positions, due to the small size of the wound, and the possibility of tying it from the arm.
The phlebotomy of the Black Veins is beneficial because it draws blood from the upper part of the head and lower body; this is because it is composed of a branch of the Basilica vein and a branch of the Cephalic vein, as we have said.
And it is fitting for the phlebotomist to take care that this vein should be above fear of the phlebotomy, since a nerve is beneath it. If then the phlebotomist adds (blood) during the incision and finds a nerve, it results in numbness, and difficult is its healing, and perhaps it will not be healed completely.
And this nerve often appears to the touch. If it is hidden in some people and is subtle and does not show itself; then you must place your phlebotomy where the incision of Alnsil is, and avoid the nerve with your strength.
If the vein is between two nerves, then find the vein in length.
Book on the Cephalic Vein. The phlebotomy of the Cephalic Vein is beneficial because it draws blood from the head and contributes to eye diseases.
And in this vein it is appropriate, if you wish, for the phlebotomist to anoint it with olive oil or myrtle when pricking it; because it is more secure than all veins, because there is no artery beneath it nor nerve.
It is fitting for you at phlebotomy that you ask the barber only the head of the arm, and seek a more gentle place: it does not harm him if it is not found with the first strike, so repeat upon it with phlebotomy several times.
Perhaps an abscess may occur in some men when it is not bled at the first strike, but it does not harm the abscess in some.
Chapter on the utility of phlebotomy and the precautions to be observed. The quality of phlebotomy and its occurrences from which it needs to be corrected should be considered by you.
Firstly, you must know that phlebotomy is not administered unless for the conservation of health, and the persistence of this, and caution against the advent of illnesses.
And phlebotomy is to be made in one of the three veins that are in the elbow, namely the Cephalica, the Fusca, and the Basilica. And phlebotomy is to be made at the beginning of spring after fasting, in summer and autumn, and during winter after eating, when signs of fullness appear.
Phlebotomy is to be made on the first or third day, after three hours have passed from the previous day.
However, phlebotomy administered in illnesses does not have a fixed hour: but when it is appropriate and necessary for that, it can be done at night or day, and at any hour, and at any time.
It is not fitting to bleed boys until they reach fourteen years of age. A pregnant woman should not be bled except from the fourth month until the seventh. Old men who have already passed sixty years should not be bled.
If anyone wishes to be bled for any reason whatsoever, it is necessary that he purifies his intestines before bleeding, if there is much fecal retention within them: so that during bleeding, the putrid superfluities from the intestines do not attract and harm the principal members.
The patient should not be bled when he has gluttony (toctina), that is, satiety or nausea: nor if drunk, nor still hungover from drunkenness, until that has been removed from them. Nor a sick person with dropsy.
And take care that bleeding is not repeated after an attack of colic, vomiting, diarrhea, increase in sexual activity, labor, exercise, wakefulness, fasting or any other thing that weakens the body's strength from a material or animal source.
Then consider in finesse the state of the humors before that (the bleeding), if the blood is thick due to foods and drinks, and medicines: if possible for him.
Then let a bath be entered, unless there is something preventing it: or exercise in some way, so that the blood may become finer.
And place the bleeding of that person in the chest on the third day, as we have said: and strive to evacuate his chest that same day from all bad animal incidents such as anxiety, anger, fear: and from all bodily incidents such as labor, punishment, superfluity and similar things.
And let him have present those things that are customary for mankind to use from aromatics, fragrances, music and similar things, in the amount possible.
Then let the blood-letter sit on a raised stool higher than the bed for bleeding: afterwards, he shall draw out his arm and rub it with his own hand twice or thrice. Then tie the ligament with a suture, and roll it twice.
And let the compression be even, because when the compression is uneven, either through excess in the compression, then it prevents the flow of blood; or through loosening, then it also prevents the flow of blood.
Then after the compression, it is necessary for the one who performs bloodletting to rub his hands together until the veins swell and become palpable.
Then the person who performs bloodletting cleans the place of bloodletting with a little oil, using an old and appropriate tool.
Then he places his index finger of the left hand over the vein at the place where he wants to perform bloodletting, slightly above it; so that the vein does not flee and the blow is cushioned. Because veins are like running strings at the place of bloodletting; and some are full of wind; and when you place a bloodletter over it, it contracts under the bloodletter, and the bloodletter is deceived, and does not open the vein; and if he opens it, it is compressed; therefore, for this reason, the bloodletter must firm his grip, and be attentive to all these matters.
Then place the bloodletter down. If then the vein is opened from one side, it is good. And if not, then repeat it on the other side at that place slightly below or above it with haste; if the spot is not pierced.
If it is pierced or the weak person shrinks back in fear, then dismiss him for a day or two; and do not tie the ligament. Perhaps an abscess attracts heat. Do not enter the bathhouse.
Then repeat the bloodletting if it is necessary.
If the opening is small or blood flows thinly; and you fear that not enough blood will flow out; then repeat the bloodletting in the same place correctly, and increase the procedure slightly.
Make it quickly before removing the location, for the location to be removed is in many people during a small operation.
If therefore you see it already removed, then do not go over it at all, for it does not excuse anything from you. And place something made of oil on top of it: because it softens the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than oil itself, and than other oils.
Similarly administer oil paste in every phlebotomy of yours, where you cut veins against the flow of blood; and they do it with Tyrian alfarach and sagzenea when placed on a spot.
For the blood is thinned and dissolved when it is thick.
If it happens in the place of phlebotomy that a large abscess occurs, which often happens, especially to him who is not a phlebotomist except for that time, or if it is an opening of small vein, then hurry and place on the spot a sponge soaked in water and salt, heated slightly; and squeeze it once an hour: because it dissolves.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood exits from the vein itself when it has completed its course, or from another vein.
If something remains in the place after several days that is black or greenish, it does not harm it; and if you wish, place on top of it something of aloes, or myrrh dissolved; or something from the juice of mint, that is, camphor or similar to it.
And abscesses and swellings often occur at the phlebotomy of the Basilica.
Therefore place your hand on it, then if you find softness under your pressure on it, then that swelling is bad. Be careful so that you place something on it from what I said. Perhaps blood from the artery will flow from it.
Make an ointment for it with that where there is a hardening, so that the place becomes firm. Then take care of it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
And it is necessary to draw out every man's blood according to the quantity of its power. And what appears as a purple color appearing on top of the blood.
If the blood is black, then allow it to come out until it turns red; and similarly if you see it thick, then allow it to become thin; and similarly if it is sharp, then allow its sharpness to disappear.
And it is necessary for the one who is full, strong, and requires bloodletting once, that the venesection of the vein be amplified, and the venesector be on the side; and for the one who is weak, it should be the opposite of this.
And it is necessary to draw it out from the veins, and that the hole be narrow; and it is better for the venesection of the vein that the incision be transverse rather than puncture.
And this method of venesection is safe from bleeding and from the cutting of nerves; and it is more praiseworthy and safer than venesection by width and length.
And for one who often comes to venesection due to fainting, it is necessary to eat before venesection with a small piece of bread soaked in water infused with dates or pomegranate; or to drink if he is heated up; and draw blood from him in three veins, or four.
And if it is a cold constitution, then before venesection let him eat bread soaked in syrup of honey or in aromatic syrup with spices, or in aromatic scented syrup.
If it happens that fainting occurs during bloodletting and the cause is abundant bleeding, then it is necessary that water of flesh be taken for drink, and a subtle perfumed syrup; and it should be used with aromatics along with alum and the chest should be wrapped with it; and the remedies mentioned in the chapter on fainting due to evacuation should also be used.
Whoever wishes to cut his arm and let out his blood in the second way: it is necessary for him whose bloodletting is due to excessive evacuation, and the strength of his blood is weak, that he lets out blood gradually by the quantity of its strength over continuous days.
Whoever wishes to cut his arm and let out his blood in the second way, and is perhaps weak: then he should do it after seven or nine hours following his first bloodletting.
Whoever wishes to draw blood from his body towards the opposite side on which it falls: it is necessary for him that it be done on the second or third day.
In whose body blood is already very hot and sharpens, causing fever: it is necessary that the blood exit from him on one occasion, and a large quantity be drawn out; and the opening should be increased until fainting occurs: after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting; and so that you place your hand on his pulse at the course of the blood, lest death occur in its place where fainting does. For it often happens when the bloodletter is ignorant and falls through negligence.
And it is not necessary when one wishes to cut the arm and let out blood in the second way, if the opening of the vein is already closed and the exit of the blood becomes difficult, that pressure be applied violently to it and it be quickly stretched open. It is very bad, rather let it be, until it is bled a second time.
Remove with sharpness the clot that is formed from blood in the vein opening, and place something of dissolved salt on it. And place upon it some tyriaca alfaroch or sagzenea, and press it gently until the blood comes out.
But if the vein has already been cut (or lanced), then let it be, and do not cover it up, until the boil settles down.
If there is need to draw blood a second time and there is no excuse, then either bleed the same place or in another vein of the other arm.
The arm is bled at the place of Fusca and Basilica, when they are not found and hidden, because it is composed of them.
And the quality of the bleeding is that a weak person should insert his hand into warm water until it turns red, and the vein appears clearly.
Then bind over it with a bandage not too tightly. Then bleed it across (transversely), gradually and not lengthwise: and let the opening be wide, and your bleeding there should be only a small one on the junction of the hands.
If the flow of blood is prevented, then immerse your hand in warm water again, and let the blood flow into the water until you have satisfied your need.
But if you are in the days of summer, then excuse yourself from immersing your hand in warm water. And most importantly, the flow of blood into warm water is put during winter time.
And this vein's bleeding is safer than all the others because it is not a pulsating vein nor a nerve.
Phlebotomy for the hand (Salvatella) of the right side, is useful for the diseases of the liver.
The quality of phlebotomy (for the hand) is that you tie off (the vein of) the hand with a ligature or with your hand, after introducing it into warm water, until the vein becomes inflamed and it is strongly felt. Then make an incision (across) the phlebotomy (vein). If you cut it completely, it will not harm him at all.
And be careful, lest you amputate the hand with the phlebotomy (vein). For under that are the nerves of the fingers exposed from the flesh.
Then put the hand again into warm water and let the blood flow out there. For if you do not repeat it (putting the hand into warm water), the blood congeals in the opening of the vein and prevents its flow.
Therefore, when you have taken out the required amount of blood from the blood, then place oil and salt on the vein so that it does not quickly coagulate.
And similarly, it is necessary that you do this with every tightened branch (vein).
However, the aid for this phlebotomy (vein of the left hand) is that it is useful for the diseases of the spleen; and similarly do as you did with the second one equally.
The aid for the saphenous vein phlebotomy is for diseases that are in the lower part of the body, such as the diseases of the uterus, and retention of menstruation, and weaknesses of the kidneys, and ulcers of the hips (pelvis) and legs old ones, and similar diseases.
The quality of the phlebotomy for the saphenous vein is that a sick person should introduce his foot into warm water; and he shall make friction on it until the veins become swollen, and tie above the ankle with thongs. The location of the vein is at the heel, appearing towards the thumb; and branches from it, on the face of the foot, many branches.
Therefore, the vein should be tapped at its larger branch or at the heel where it collects; since it is better and safer.
If one were to tap the vein on the sole of the foot, then take care to avoid the nerves that are below it on the surface of the foot.
And place your lancet for tapping the vein across it, as if you were going to cut it; and let the tapper be named Nesil.
If there is a prohibition of blood flow, then immerse his foot in warm water; and release the blood to run into it until it has drained out.
If the tapper misses the vein in the first attempt, then he should return with the lancet to the upper part, for it is safe and there is no harm, as long as you are careful of the nerves, as we have said.
And likewise, with the Saphenous vein of the other foot equally.
The location of the Sciatic vein, as we have said, is at the heel from the forest side.
And the aid for this tapper is, in pain of the ankle, when it is due to hot blood.
And the quality of this tapper is that he should enter the bath when sick and hurry; and bind his leg from the side of the ankle to above the heel with a thin, long plaster; for it does not appear unless for that reason.
When it appears, then tap the vein according to whatever skill is possible for you; or across it; and it is better, either that you cut it with an incision, or sever it with a section. The place [of the ailment] is very harmful, and it is hidden in many people to a great degree.

Then dissolve the narrowing [of the vein], and place skin over the place of the incision, and tighten the place. It is cured quickly.
On Ventosation, and the quality of its use.
They [ventoses] are made from horns, copper, and glass.
And ventosation is done in two ways. With scarification and bloodletting; the other is ventosation without scarification.
And this ventosation which is without scarification, is done in two ways. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And the places on the body where ventosation with scarification and bloodletting are administered are fourteen. One of them is the ventosation of Alnocrates, and it is at the back of the head; Alkehel, and it is in the middle of the neck; ventosation of Alhagdahim, and they are two plates on the neck from both sides; ventosation of the brain, and it is under the lower jaw on the inside of the mouth; ventosation of two spoons; ventosation of Alachosos above the end of the tail; ventosation of two nails, and they are in the middle of the arms; ventosation of two legs; ventosation of two ankles.
And ventosation does not extract blood from the veins scattered throughout the flesh, and for that reason, it does not have the same effectiveness as in phlebotomy (bloodletting). It is not necessary to administer fumigation or with fire, nor without fire for any illness caused by congestion, until the entire body has been evacuated.
If necessity arises due to illness or custom for fumigation, it is administered at the beginning, end, and middle of each month, and at any time whenever necessary.
Because there are people who, when their blood multiplies, find a heaviness and swelling in the head, and a sore throat.
And from these people there are some who find congestion and redness on their face, head, and neck. And from these people there are some who find itching on their face and forehead, darkness, and dullness in their eyes.
And from these people there are some who scratch the place of their fumigation. And from these people there is one whose laughter increases. And from these people there is one who finds a taste of blood in their mouth: or the tongue is scraped and the blood is spitted out. And from these people there is one whose sleep increases. And from these people there is one who sees blood and redness, dead people, wounds, and things similar to these in their sleep.
When we see something from these people, especially during the middle of the third month: then we command fumigation to be performed after two or three hours have passed since the day.
The benefit of fumigation to the neck is: because it relieves heaviness in the head and what drains from it goes to the eyes. But it must be after the evacuation of the entire body.
And this fumigation is in place of cephalic phlebotomy (bloodletting from the head).
It is not permitted to use [it] on one who has a cold constitution, or [on] one who has a catarrh. It causes him great harm.
And for this reason, it is not appropriate to use [it] on the elderly and on one who has cold diseases of the head.
He who is constant over it generates forgetfulness in him.
And for this reason, you must instruct the Windfinder to lower his hand with the wind for a moment towards the lower parts, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
Windfinding or Alkehel is in place of phlebotomy (Fuscus), and phlebotomy (Basilica), and for this reason it causes coughing, constriction of the breath, panting of the instrument, coughing, and congestion.
It is necessary to press Windfinder Alkehel briefly, because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
However, Windfinding Alhagdahim causes accidents of pain in the head, migraines, hemigraine, perforations, and pain in the roots of the teeth; and it is in place of phlebotomy (Basilica).
And you must instruct the Windfinder not to press his hand during scarification, so as not to cut an artery, and there is bleeding.
However, Windfinding under the chin causes alcohol in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and similar diseases that are in the mouth; and it is in place of phlebotomy elsewhere, which are at the two lips.
However, Windfinding with a spatula causes alchafacha, which is caused by flexion and heat.
However, Windfinding of the internal parts of two forks causes help for phlebotomy of the three veins (Basilica, Fuscus, Cephalica); because it draws blood from all those small veins that are in the flesh; and these small veins draw blood from other larger veins until the attraction reaches the three veins.
It is necessary that you warn the one who lances, not to proceed too much in scarification; because the place is stripped of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and veins.
The one who ventose (the anal area) applies to hemorrhoids of the anus and to lower ulcers.
It is necessary that you warn the one who ventose (the anal area) to have a large ventose and made of air; because the place needs strong suction and perhaps the delicate vents break; and it should be incised with a large scarification.
The diminution of the legs' ventose is evident; because they draw blood from the whole body, and cause old pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder; they provoke menstruation, bothor, and ademenul; and stand at the place of phlebotomy for two saphenas and alhorcobeni.
Nevertheless they weaken the body much, and cause fainting in many men.
And the help of anal ventose's alhorcobeni is near the help of anal ventose of two legs.
The quality of the position of ventose is that you put the ventose first in an empty place and suck with moderate suction; and do not prolong the position of the ventose, but place them quickly and remove them quickly, so that humors come to the place at equal arrival; and do not stop doing this and continuing until you see the place already red and swollen, and the redness appears as blood; then scarify, and repeat suction gradually. Then consider in the arrangement of bodies.
For the one who is among men has tender flesh and rare pores, it is necessary that you incise him once, not more; so that the place does not become ulcerated.
And instruct the Ventosator to enlarge the scarification and make it shallow; and regulate the suction in ease, speed, and gentle motion.
If there is thickening in the blood, then it is necessary to scarify it twice. In the first incision, an opening is made for the subtlety of the blood and its thinning; and in the second examination of the detailed extraction of thick blood.
If the blood is extremely foul, then the scarification should be made for a third time; to follow what comes last.
And when we wish to draw out blood little by little or in small quantity, one scarification is sufficient for us. If we want the extraction of much blood, we shall make many scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is thick; then it is necessary to scarify with deep scarifications; and the end equal in scarification, is the depth of the skin.
What is that which should be applied from the oils for the position of the Ventosator, and what the cautions are for the one who blows.
In the one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and the pores of which are closed, it is necessary to anoint the places for blowing with oils that are opening, softening, resolving; if it is in summer, such as alkali oil or volatile oil or sweet almond oil or cucumber seed oil.
If it is winter time, then use oil of narcissus, or oil of lily, or oil of chamomile, or ointment: and similar things.
If excess is thick and cold, then use oil of amaracis, or oil of balsam, or oil of aloe, or oil of dittany: and similar things.
If the nosebleed is from large pores, of tender flesh: then it is necessary to forbid oil, and it is necessary that they rinse those areas after nosebleeding with rose water, or cold water, or salt water, or water of pumpkin, or water of purslane: and similar things.
He whose blood is of much moisture, rinse the nosebleed areas with vinegar, or with water of myrtle, and sumac: and similar things.
He whose excesses are thick, rinse the nosebleed areas with old wine, or with water of sage, or decoction of dittany, or chamomile: and similar things.
It is necessary to avoid nosebleed in the bath, or near the bath, indeed it is necessary to administer it after leaving the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
Nor should anyone sleep after a nosebleed.
Chapter on: What is required to control nosebleed or phlebotomy before a nosebleed.
And afterwards it is necessary to consider first: if nosebleed or phlebotomy patient is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation prevail over his blood, then it is necessary for him to receive cooling agents, such as grape must: also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and secaniabin, and iuleb: and similar things.

Chapter on Venesection without Scarification. Venesections that are without scarification are those placed on the liver, spleen, podagra, on the abdomen, umbilicus, and places of kidneys, and ankles; because these members do not endure scarification upon them.
Nor does it seek through these (veins), unless an attraction of blood from one member to another; as we place the venesection on the breasts in disease of the flow of blood in the nose. And we use them to remove the venosity of an unpleasant cold in the limb; as we place the venesection on the abdomen and umbilicus. For it makes the limb rare, and warms it up, and removes pain for this reason, because it has resolved that venosity.
Also placed over the kidneys when occur there abscess or stone. By its virtue of attraction perhaps it opens an abscess, or removes the stone from its place.
And similarly when it is placed over the spleen, and on both sides for the shaking of the liver or spleen due to wind.
These shakings are administered empty when there is just cutting. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered filled with warm water in cases of pleurisy: and that is to fill the Shaking, and it should be large, warm water alone, or water in which cooked herbs are steeped that agree.
Form of the Shaking administered with fire.
The width of its opening should be two fingers apart, as we have determined; and the depth should be half a palm; and on one side there should be a small hole, according to what size the needle enters it; it should be made of copper or citrine, smooth, equal edges, polished; so that it does not harm the body while placed on top of it; and there should be a transverse bar across it in the middle, made of iron or wood, where the candle is placed with fire.
And now this Shaking is made large, larger than we have described, and not much smaller than that, according to the sickness and age of the one who uses it.
The Shakings for children and weak ones are different from those for men and large bodies.
The quality of placing this Shaking with fire on a body is to burn linen with fire properly, or a small candle from wax; and place it on the middle bar that is in the middle of the Shaking; so that the fire is lit near the lower part of the Shaking, not to burn the weak body.
Then let it be placed on the limb, and a finger should be over the hole that I mentioned, until when we hold the abscess; while we need, remove the finger, and the abscess will exit through the hole, and the abscess will immediately burst.
Then let the linen be heated according to its manner, and repeat it if necessary.
In the abscess applied to the disease of pleurisy with water, there is no rod [or probe] crossed, nor hole; it is not given except because it is filled with water and placed on the limb only.
And this is its form.
And this abscess, the larger it is, so that it can hold much water, is better.
Chapter on Suspension of Sugar-of-Lead.
Chapter CXIX. Sanguisugae are not given in many cases except where the position of abscesses is not possible due to their smallness, such as lips, gums, and similar places. Or on a bare member, such as a finger, and nose, and similar places.
And the quality of using them is: that you come from Sanguisugas which are in sweet, clean waters, free from decay.
Then they should be released day and night into sweet water until they are hungry, and there remains nothing inside them. Then the body is evacuated first with phlebotomy, and with abscesses. Then wash the sick member until it turns red. Afterwards, they are placed upon it.
When they are filled and fall out, and it is possible to suck the place with an abscess, then it is more helpful for treatment; and if not, then clean the place with vinegar. Then with much water, it is rubbed, and squeezed out.
If the course of blood persists after a bleeding, and it is oozing: then immerse a linen cloth in cold water, and place it on top until the oozing stops.
If the bleeding is abundant, then sprinkle powdered zechium (a type of alum), or gallnuts, or similar substances from the styptic plants upon it until the bleeding stops.
Or place half-peeled beans on the location and let them remain until they stick to the area, as the bleeding will stop.
And if it is necessary to repeat the bleeding (bleeding the patient again), it is advisable to change them whenever possible.
If bleeding is forbidden by suspension (a practice where a tourniquet was used to stop the blood flow), then clean the site with fresh blood. Or insert a lance into the sites until something comes out from the blood. Then place them (the instruments), for when they feel something coming from the blood, they will stick immediately.
When you want them to fall (the clots), sprinkle something from crushed aloes, salt or ashes over them and they will fall immediately.
This chapter is also often necessary in the art of medicine and it is the restoration of broken bones and dislocations that occur in bones.
Know that the foolish doctors and common people boast about this chapter and those who have never examined an ancient book or read a single letter from it.
And because of this reason, the method is rare among our scholars on earth. And I have never encountered a good example of this method and I have not acquired that knowledge from it except for the length of reading ancient books and my dedication to understanding them until I extracted that knowledge from them.
Then I attach myself by experience and study to the length of my life.
And now I have described to you in this Chapter everything that contains my knowledge: and beyond what my experience has passed, after I have abbreviated it for you, and freed it from the occupation of elongation, and abbreviated it at the end of abbreviation, and shown it at the end of declination: and I have formed many shapes in it from the shapes of the instruments that are administered in it, as I did in two preceding Chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary that we say in the prologue of this Chapter, the sums and chapters, to whose understanding the craftsman must be guided firstly on the truth of them: namely, so that you, and those prompt, may know or learn this noble art from it, and others from you.
I say therefore, that when a fracture or dislocation or twisting or falling happens to someone, then it is necessary that you rush firstly to bleed him, and to dissolve himself, or both at the same time: if something does not prevent it, such as weakness of virtue; or it is he who has happened something of these things, a boy or an old man worn out; or it is a time of violent heat, or very violent cold.
Then he should be content with his food, cold vegetables, and the flesh of beans and lentils; and forbid wine, thick meats, and to be filled from foods and every food that fills the veins with blood. Until you are secure from a hot abscess and no longer fear the outflow of matter to the member, then let the sick return to their first regimen with which their custom was accustomed.
When the fractured bone begins to heal, then it is necessary that the sick be fed with nutritious food in great quantity, thick, heavy, which contains viscosity; such as food made from boiled wheat and water, rice, heads of cattle, feet, abdomens of sheep, eggs, fresh fish, thick wine and similar things. For this diet makes the coagulation of the fracture quicker and better.
And you should know that fractured bones, when they are in men who are in the first old age and decrepit, it is not possible for them to be held together and consolidated according to their nature always, due to the drying out of their limbs; and they are consolidated and held together which come from them at the end of softness, like bones of small boys.
Nevertheless, nature connects something similar to a glue around the fractured bone from all sides, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and tightens it until they are conglutinated on both sides, and bound together until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until it does not prohibit anything from its operations; and for this reason it is necessary that you place nutriment for the sick food which has thickness, viscosity and heaviness, as we have said.
And you should know that the species of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of limbs; since a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the head bone; and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the backbone; and similarly all other limbs are diverse among themselves; and I come with the reminder of every species of fractures explained in its own chapter. The species of bone fracture are also diversified within themselves.
Since the fracture is bow-shaped, there are also stellate occurrences in it. (In this context "stellate" refers to a star-like pattern or configuration.) And sometimes the fracture occurs along the length of the bone; and these are its stellate or star-like fractures, and extensions proceeding, just as they are in a reed, when it is cut with a knife for writing purposes. (In this context "stellate" refers to a star-like pattern or configuration.) And not proceeding; and there is a fracture with wound and disruption at the head, and there is a small crack fracture.
And every species has a skill or its own specific tool for its restoration, according to how it will be remembered, distinguished in their respective places.
And from that which the bone fracture is known, there is its tortuosity and elevation; and its appearance to the sense, and the sound it makes when you compress it with your hand.
When therefore there is no tortuosity appearing in the place, nor a sound when you touch the bone with movement, nor does it produce a great pain; then there is no fracture there, indeed it may be a tortuosity or a small crack; therefore you should not move it with your hand and compression at all, instead put medicines on top of it, according to how it will be remembered, after a suitable time: then it is strained by a ligature made finely. (In this context, "ligature" refers to a medical bandage or suture used for binding wounds together.)
And know that a place becomes arched and is split into two, except there are stellate occurrences in it; however, all parts should be moved away from their comparison: then you must begin from your hour for its correction, equalizing it before a hot abscess occurs to it. (In this context "stellate" refers to a star-like pattern or configuration.)
If a boil occurs warm, then let it be left for some days until the boil subsides. Then cut (the boil) according to whatever method can be done with kindness and skill.
And know that the healing (of the wound) and its setting (in order), is easier than of a bone, in which there are fractures.
Then suture it, according to what comes to your mind regarding its healing.
If there are fractures in the bone, then it is necessary to stretch this broken limb by two parts, whether it be a hand or a foot; or with your hand, if the member is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and your hand.
Let the position you place the limb on be equal to its natural shape on a flat surface, until the parts of the broken limb extend; then try with all your skill and in any way possible to bring back these additions (to their places); and be careful according to your strength so that no pain or injury occurs due to your operation, nor damage; and try according to your strength to apply one bone to its companion, according to the best forms.
And it is necessary that you try and touch it with your hand at that hour. If you see something irregular there, correct it, and set (the bone) straight according to your ability.
And avoid violent extension and strong compression, as many fools do. For often they make a boil occur due to their operation, or contraction in the limb; as we have already seen from their operations many times.
Then bind (the limb) after the setting, and a proper operation, and a tightening of the member, and quiet, and calm; and warn the patient about it. The translation of the given Latin sentence is: "And make him take care not to move it during his hours of sleep, and his sleep, and his restlessness, and during his digestion, and all its movements with utmost strength. "And let him take care that the position of the member which follows pain is not maintained. "What is meant by this, is that when it feels pain or injury in the position of a member, then it must be moved to another position from which it does not feel pain.
"And let him take care that his position is even and straight so that no deformity occurs in the member when it is being restored.
"And the quality of the immobilization of a fractured member, as I have said.
"Know that fractured members are classified according to their size, dimension, and shape.
"Those which are small such as the arm, finger, club foot, and similar, then it should be wrapped in a soft, fine fabric. "But those which are large such as the thigh and leg, there should be a wide, hard wrapping.
"Because a broad bandage unites a large member and compresses it equally from all sides with an equal immobilization so that no mark penetrates.
"Then begin to apply a suitable ointment after evacuation on the equal part, on a soft cotton over the location of the fracture. "Then begin to wrap the bandage over the location of the fracture with three or four layers, depending on the member's deserving, and tighten your hand slightly with the bandage.
"Then go with him to the upper part from the location of the fracture and loosen less than your previous immobilization on the fractured location. Then prolong yourself slightly with a slight rotation from the place of the fracture, and gradually soften the constriction until you seize a large amount from a healthy place.
Then take another poultice and apply it again over the broken place with repeated rotations. Go downwards, with a slight rotation, to the lower part from the fracture. And make your operation in the constriction of the rotation and its softening, according to what we said in the first rotation above.
Then put between the rotations (bandages) of soft wool or cloth, which matches the curvature of the fracture, if there is a curvature in it; and if not, do not place anything on it.
Then apply another poultice over it (the bandages). Then place equal stars (small poultices) evenly over these rotations, immediately if there is no swelling or a hot abscess in the area.
If there is swelling or an abscess in the area; then place over it what sits on that abscess and removes the swelling; and let it be for some days. Then bind over these small poultices (stars).
And let the small poultices (stars) be from the middle parts of wide reeds, shaved and wisely formed; or they can be from wood where sieves are made, which are made from pine, yew, palm branches, or calingi; and let the width of all the poultices be according to this form and this same shape.
However, it is necessary that the poultice placed over the fracture be slightly larger and wider than the others; and the length of the poultice should be according to what is suitable for the limb in size and smallness.
Then bind over the poultices with another poultice, according to the first constriction (bandage). Then bind above with threads properly, as we have said regarding the stricture: and it is, that your stricture be over the place of the fracture more: and as it is more elongated from the fracture, the stricture is lesser.
It is necessary that the threads be of a medium size and fineness, and be made of soft linen.
Since if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the operation of foolish ones placing their threads from twisted cords of coarse linen, it is a great mistake: since the stricture falls with them out of quality.
And fine threads again do not fit: since you will not achieve with the stricture what you want.
It is not necessary that there be less than a finger's breadth between the awl and the awl.
If the weak one is hurt by the tips of the awls after the stricture in healthy places: then place under them with wool felt or carded wool, until nothing is hurt from it.
When there is a fracture with a wound, and it happens that the break occurs in the skin, I will come with the reminder of this in the second chapter concerning special cases.
And know that not every fractured member needs to be bound with awls from the first day. Because when the member is large, it is not necessary to place upper awls on it, unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety from the risk of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters, which the ancients placed on a fracture and dislocation.
¶ Common method of plasters abbreviated, restoring the fracture, suitable for multiple constitutions, especially women and children: since it is from that which heat does not conquer nor cold.
And it is to receive from the flour mill powder, and it is the marrow of the wheat that hangs on the walls of the mill near the movement of the millstone: and make it as follows, except you sift it with egg white: and place the mixture of it not thick, nor thin. Then use it.
Another method of plaster (bandage), for setting a fracture, dislocation and twisting. Prepare almess, laudanum, acacia, enula, and mugath; and boil all of them with 10 parts of myrrh, 5 parts of each aloes, and 20 parts of alethil. Let everything be ground and sifted, then mix it with water of alethil or with sheep's milk white if the bandage is heated for a sick person. Then use this plaster (bandage). For it is of good composition, quickly sets broken bones and helps many people.
Another method of plaster (bandage), which also helps with a fracture and twisting. Prepare mugath, mess, white alteae, all with 10 parts of myrrh, 5 parts of each aloes, 6 parts of acacia, and 20 parts of ground Armenian pitch. Let everything be ground well and sifted, then boil it with water or sheep's milk white, and administer (the mixture).
Another method of plaster (bandage) for joints and bones separated from their places; it relieves pain and swelling, and heals a broken bone. Wool grease is collected, immersed in vinegar and boiled oil; it is placed on the location.
And indeed, there is no virtue of healing in this plaster (bandage), but it is good for the healing of hot abscesses; and it expels pain specifically.
Another method of plaster (bandage) that sets a broken bone. Take the leaves of wild fig and the leaves of silvestrous papaver; grind them all and make a plaster from both.
Another method of shortened plaster (bandage), administered for the healing of a broken bone; and when you want the removal of the remaining abscess. Prepare a mixture of the root of the marshmallow plant (altheae), chamomile, herb violet flowers, and barley flour, all in equal parts: grind them all and mix them with wine, if the affected member is not already heated.
If the affected member is already heated, then prepare it with water from moist coriander seeds, or simply use this preparation.
Another stronger method of plaster for resolution, stronger than the first, when there is a hard abscess during restoration of a member. Prepare a mixture of the root of marshmallow plant (altheae), flax seeds, beechwood resin, licorice, sesame seeds, asafetida, violet flowers and chamomile, all in equal parts: grind them all and mix with water of willow, sweet water or wine. Apply this preparation to the affected member according to its heat and the calmness of the heat.
Hippocrates did not say in his book to apply on a broken member during its restoration unless it is made of beeswax and oil; he does not mention anything else, and he said that it should be between coarseness and subtlety.
Galenus saw it fit that substances with a heating property be applied on a broken member during its restoration, such as myrrh, aloes, frankincense and similar ones from the substances mentioned earlier.
Another plaster method for injuries and pain relief. Prepare a mixture of mugwort, chickpeas, human hair (either cut or feathers of birds), marshmallow root and salt in equal parts: grind and sift it, and make plaster out of this mixture.
The amount that should remain tied (the ligature) is as follows: consider if there is no pain or itching, or the bone does not move from its place; if so, do not untie it for several days. If it happens to him at the place of itching intensely, or distressing pain, or swelling: then hurry up and dissolve it within an hour, and remove the plaster from him; and take a soft cloth or a soft sea sponge, moist; and immerse it in warm water, and wash the place with it until the itching ceases and the pain subsides.
If it happens to him at the place of itching intensely, or distressing pain, or swelling: then hurry up and dissolve it within an hour, and remove the plaster from him; and take a soft cloth or a soft sea sponge, moist; and immerse it in warm water, and wash the place with it until the itching ceases and the pain subsides. Then let the member rest for an hour. Then place on it a soft woolen cloth, immersed in vinegar and oil, or rose oil; and bind over it at night until you are safe from hot infection, and the swelling of the member subsides, and the pain disappears.
Then repeat on it a subtle bandage and small plaster; do not bind it with your first compression and fine ones, until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling and all the other occurrences have been removed entirely, and you need plaster and bandage, then repeat it as you did at first, equally.
If nothing of what we mentioned happens to the member, then do not dissolve it before three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is released after twenty days, according to what you see from the dissolution of the member as we said, until a fracture adheres and coagulation of flesh grows over it: add also at that time more than your first bandage in the bandage; and add also in the thickening of food, according to what was previously remembered.
If you see that the place of the fracture is already drying out and thinning more than necessary: then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Heat the embrocation with warm water in every place where you dissolve it, and let it be in all three days, and apply a ligature slightly.
Through this operation, nourishment flows to the member, and it heals quickly.
But what the foolish restorers do in place of fractured members when it is not first restored as required, and it is restored according to a curve, is an error from their operation, and great fear: and if it were correct, they would have said this in their ancient books, and operated with it.
And I never saw any trace of them [the error] in this at all, and it is better not to do that.
Chapter on the making of a fracture in the head.
The types of fractures are various, and their shapes are numerous; and their causes are distinct.
For a fracture is that which occurs by striking with a sword: and striking removes the bone to the extent it reaches the syphac (which is under the bone), just as an axe does in wood: and for this reason, this type of fracture is called an Axial fracture.
Or the sword cuts something of the bone and cuts its face, but does not cut through with the incision to the end of it: and this type of fracture is called a Calaan (meaning an Absolute Lifting).
And there are two types of wounds, either large or small.
And from a fracture is one which is crushed or rubbed: and the cause of this is a blow with a stone, or falling on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also one that penetrates and approaches the pannicle (which is under the bone): or it is on the face of the bone: and this type of wound is also among those two types, either small or large.
And from a break is that which is hidden within the bone in the subtlety of hair; and it is a small fissure, and because of this it is called this kind of fracture Capillary.
And from these (things) is a fracture that happens from an accident or impact of a stone and similar things which cause a lamina of the bone to enter within; and it makes a hollow place: just as it happens to the bellows of air, when they are struck, and part of them enters within.
And indeed, this (kind of fracture) is most often found in the moist bones of the head, such as those of children.
And all these kinds of fractures (are accompanied by) swelling and separation; and I shall return with a reminder of the whole in its place.
And we know all these kinds of fractures by the examination of them and investigation with probes, and removal of rotten flesh that is on top of them.
The Capillary kind (of fracture) is recognized by this, that it appears on the bone, and is scraped away, and a ligature is applied on top of it with pitch: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, consider first the condition of the patient; then if you see that there are indications of illness from the accidents (symptoms) themselves, as in cholera vomiting, extension, loss of intellect, loss of voice, fainting, fever, eye discharge or redness, similar symptoms in other disorders, it will certainly not heal: and if you see the patient's condition is not terrifying and you hope for recovery; then begin its treatment.
If someone comes to you with a wound, and it is a fracture, it is during the winter days; then it is necessary that you study its excision (removal of bone) before the fourteenth day according to every situation.
If it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before it becomes rotten under the bone from the fat, and such accidents occur as we have described.
If the fracture of the bone has already reached the fat touching the brain, and is with particles and traction: then it is necessary that you cut the fractured and smoothed part, according to what I have told you.
It is necessary that you shave the head of the wounded: and bore into the bone according to whatever method is possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is light on the weak.
If while boring into the bone there comes a flow of blood or a hot abscess: then oppose it with what is necessary. It is necessary that you fill the place with soaked rags in wine and rose oil: and let the wound rest until the abscess settles, and be secure from bleeding. Then begin in the treatment of the bone, and its removal.
And this is done according to one of two methods.
One method is that you cut the bone with a sharp chisel: and this is its shape.
Then administer another chisel wider than the first: and this is its shape.
Then administer yet another chisel wider still.
And the second method is that you have a number of chisels of different sizes; some of which are wider than others, and some shorter than others: and they should be at the end of their sharpness, and made from iron or steel: and use them with ease when striking the chisel, so as not to strike the head, and hurt it.
If the bone is hard and firm: then it is necessary that you bore around its circumference before you administer Incision, with Terebris which are called Terebras, not penetrating too deeply; and they are not named thus, except because they do not pass beyond the bone's meaty limit to that which is behind it; for this reason, because the Terebrum has a rounded extremity above what is beneath its head and a sharp point below, similar to an edge, and a small circle prevents immersion, and passing through the bone's thickness.
It is necessary for you to take a large number of these Terebris, one of which should correspond to the thickness of the bone; until the present is to you all the Terebrum of the skull; whose sharp edge's length and width should be according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of Terebris or Trepans.
The quality of boring around the bone that is broken, is: you place the Terebrum on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know the bone has been penetrated. Then change the Terebrum to another place and place what is between every hole of the same thickness as a radius, or approximately so.
Then cut with Incision what is between every hole from the bone; and do it more delicately than you can above that from the edge of thinness, as we have said, until you root out the bone, either with your hand or with some other instruments which you have prepared for that purpose: such as tenaliae and delicate forceps.
And it is necessary to take care with every caution that the Terebrum or Incision does not touch anything from the siphac.
When you are boring around the bone and it is separated from the siphac, if it is continuous with it and you extract it, it is necessary to root, and cut off an equal end of what remains in the bone with another similar instrument, like the Incision. It is necessary that it (the instrument) be thin and delicate more than other incising instruments.
If something remains of small bones and cartilage: then take it easily with what you have at hand from your instruments. Then care for the wound after that with linen strips, and ointments as I said.
Another method of operation is easy, completely remote from fear; which Galen said, and praised greatly; and this is his speech. You first must begin to expose the bone fragment from the place where the fracture is more severe and more noticeable; until you have exposed that part, and placed under it the end of this concave incising instrument, which has this shape.
The concave part does not cut anything; the sharp part of it on its two sides going along in length, as you see; so that the concave part be attached to the splint, the face of which the sharp part incises into the bone.
Then strike on the incising instrument from one side with a small hammer, until you cut through the entire bone easily, as it turns; and be careful that no cloth accidentally falls into it, which you fear entirely.
And if the physician were not more foolish than humans, and less ignorant of his art, nor asleep, then if something remained attached to the bone from the cloth in certain places, he would cut it off with the end of the concave incising instrument, and remove it easily. It is freed from it without harm and without fear.
If the bone fracture does not penetrate to the cloth, and what is cut away from the bone is the face of it, and roughness and delicate cartilage remain in it; then it is necessary that you remove that roughness, and excavate those delicate cartilages with fine rasps, which you have prepared in various quantities, so that it is possible for you to apply more suitable ones in every place, and the ones themselves are straighter, according to what leads you in the operation, and the fractured bone and its shape. And it is necessary that you administer according to your proportion, firstly cut the bone, more widely than the width of those scrapers. Then use (the instrument) after that with a finer one.
And it is necessary that you administer according to your proportion, firstly cut the bone, more widely than the width of those scrapers. Then use (the instrument) after that with a finer one. And do not cease doing it in continuity until you reach the point of administering thinner and finer than all of them.
For each of the remaining small cracks and subtle breaks, it is necessary to administer care according to that which leads to its rectification.
And there is a thing that cannot be hidden upon all who have any interest in this art and it stands upon that which we have written and shown for large breaks.
If however a small piece of skin is found while you are cutting bone: then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth either doubled or tripled and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it on the first cloth: and do that as easily as possible above it, so that it does not become heavy.
Then administer above it a wide ligature: and do not tighten it except to the size that holds the wound only: and leave it with this care for one day or two days until you are secure from hot inflammation. Then dissolve it, and administer afterwards medications with which excessive dryness is necessary: such as the roots of lilies, and oat flour, and turpentine resin, and aristolochia, and similar to these: and there is, so that you make a powder from these medicines, which is powdered over the wound like dry ones.
And universally administer in it every medication according to the property of which is to cleanse and not to irritate. Be careful according to your virtue, so that the wound may be clean during your treatment: neither should there be dirt from ointment, nor dirtiness.
Be careful according to your virtue, so that the wound may be clean during your treatment: neither should there be dirt from ointment, nor dirtiness. You must not let pus gather on it at all, because when pus gathers on the brain tissue, it corrupts it and makes it rot, and after that a great mischief happens to the sick person: and it has already happened in the head wound, when the bone is cleaned by him, especially when care of it is neglected, blackness appears on its surface.
Therefore, if what I have said happens to the sick person, know that it is certainly dangerous.
If blackness does not happen except from the medicine placed upon it and this medicine is of its nature to cause blackening, then it is necessary that you take one part from honey and three parts from rose oil and wash it with these. Then place it on the wound: afterward take care of it with the remedies suitable for healing until it heals.
Chapter on the repair of a broken nose.
Know that the nose is not broken except by both of its upper parts together or one of them: therefore, because there are two bones in it, the lower part is cartilaginous and is not broken, nor does anything happen to it except abrasion, curvature, and deviation.
If one of its two parts is broken, then it is necessary that you insert a small finger into the nose and adjust the broken part with your index finger and thumb on the outside until the nose returns to its natural shape; and this should be done skillfully and easily. And be careful that no pain happens to the sick person during your treatment.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and does not reach it with the finger: then it is necessary that the end of the radius be leveled, where there is a small swelling.
If the fracture is in two parts, then make it similar (to what?); and approach with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, at a place of warm plasters (apostematis).
Then insert a piece of linen from a linen cloth into the end of the nose if the fracture is in one part; or insert two pieces of linen if the fracture is in two parts: and let there be a swelling on the linen of such a size that it fills the nostril.
And some of the ancient restorers said that linen should be soaked in butter and changed every day. But that does not seem to me; rather, it should be soaked in egg white, made with flour mill powder. Then release the linen when the bone has hardened and the cartilage has become hard.
And sometimes a feather of a goose is inserted into the nose where the linen was, once a soft cloth has been laid over it: and this serves to hold the fracture in the nose more strongly and to prevent weak ones from being prevented from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you want, do it; if you want, do the linen.
If it happens again that the nose gets an abscess while working on a warm plaster, apply a nasal embrocation with pitch, or with cotton soaked in rose oil, or a little bit of zinc oxide.
If no abscess occurs, then it is necessary to apply warm plasters with wheat flour and powdered incense mixed with egg white. Then place a soft cloth on top of it (the wound), and do not tie the nose with anything at all.
If however the bones of the nose are broken in a small fracture or crushed, then it is necessary that you cut through them and extract them with instruments suitable for the purpose. Then suture (the wound) and take care of it with something that promotes healing, such as appropriate plasters or ointments.
If the wound is between the nose, then it is necessary to take care of it with linen and administer a lead cannula until it heals.
Chapter on the restoration of the lower jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and there is no wound associated with the fracture: examine it then to see if the fracture is only on the outside and not split into two, and curved towards the inside; in this case, recognition of the injury is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, insert your left index finger into the weak jaw; similarly, if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert your right index finger; and push with it the bulge of the fracture to the inside with ease towards the exterior using your other hand from the inner part of the bone correctly, and with equal pressure.
If the fracture of the jaw has already been separated into two parts, then it is necessary to administer a traction from both parts according to right angle; until it becomes possible to align them.
If however it has already occurred that the teeth are displaced or disrupted: then tie them with a gold, silver or silk thread. Then place a compress on the broken jaw. Then place a double cloth on the jaw and position a large star-shaped pad made of it centrally over the cloth or an equal piece of sole leather along the length of the jaw.
Then bind it above, as your ligature for it is prepared, and its application fits well until it does not disintegrate; and command the sick one to be at rest and in peace; and place soft foods for his purges.
If you deem that something changes in the figure by some means: then hurry, and dissolve it on the third day. Then correct what has been altered from it, and plaster it with flour of mill, with egg white, or with wheat flour, after you have removed the soot; and place a soft wool on top of the plaster.
While that plaster remains attached to it and the bone arrangement does not change, leave it be until it heals; or tie the fracture. For this fracture is often tied in three weeks.
If in the meantime a hot abscess occurs, then administer what I have said many times for its soothing until that abscess is removed.
And if there is a fracture with a wound, be aware of it. For if the fragment separates from the bone or a tendon, then be subtle in this matter so that you remove those fragments of the tendon together with what suits you for their removal using your instruments.
If the opening of the wound is narrowed, then enlarge it with a leech according to the amount needed for your purposes.
Then when you remove the tendon and nothing remains of it, or if the opening of the wound is wide: and if not, place on it plasters that suit for this purpose, and let it heal until it heals.
Chapter on the repair of a Fork, when it breaks.
The most common break of a Fork occurs from the front part of the shoulder blade, and its fracture is made in one of three ways.
For it is broken and separated into two, except that there occur in it pustules: and it is easier for its restoration.
For such as occur in the bone, pustules: and it is difficult for its restoration.
For such as is a break with a wound.
And the operation in it when there is a break without a wound, is that you make present two servants, one of whom holds the aid following the broken beam, and the other stretches the neck towards the side.
Then with your fingers make equal the break until its form is made, according to what is necessary; and there is not a projection or a hollow in it.
If you need more extension, then it is necessary that you put under the small of the sick person a ball made from cloth or wool; and its size should be according to your necessity; and stretch, raise, and press the ball with your hand until you make equal the break, according to what is necessary.
If you cannot pull the end of the beam outwards because it has come to a depth; then the sick person must lie down on his neck; and put under his shoulder a small board in size, and press the servant's shoulder towards below until the bone of the beam which is in the depth is raised upwards; and then straighten the break, and make equal it with your fingers.
If however you feel a pustule from the beam and it moves; then it is necessary that you pierce it over it, and draw out the pustule with ease.
If however it is retained in the bone; then you make an opening in its incision with one of the cutters which you have prepared for that purpose, after you put under the beam the instrument which protects the saw-horse; and the instrument is made of wood or iron. And this is its form.
It is to be assimilated to a snail shell without concavity.
And let its width be according to that which you need for it in proportion to the size of the bone and its smallness. The length [of it] also, as far as possible for you, for the operation.
And it should have two ends, as you see; and one end shall be wide, and the other narrow.
If however the wound which you cut when you extracted the splinter of bone is wide, and it gapes open; and there is no adhesion from hot cautery; then collect two lips of the wound with suturing.
And if the wound is small, or you fear hot cautery; then fill the wound with cloths, and with wool according to the size of the wound incision.
If it happens that hot cautery occurs, then soak the cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and put it on top of it.
But the suture of a bone without wound and without cutting is, that you place on it an ointment made from milled flour with white egg yolk. And place soft wax over it. Then place a sphere under the fracture if you need it. Then take a hematic, that is, the wheel which the Arabs hold, which should be very long; and its width should be one palm or approximately that. Then place folded wool over soft wax and ointment. Then take a needle from thin wood, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then pass it through the cloth. Then pass the cloth with the needle into the place of the hematic which falls upon the fracture and into the wound.
