Then place the hematic upon the fracture as it is rolled, and pass it over his neck, and under the healthy part and under the sick part; and draw it over every part, and so that the suture holds the fracture tight; and this is what is not hidden from you, and all of it, so that the needle does not remove the fractured bone. It is necessary to observe the sick person every day. Whenever the bandage becomes soft, and you see the knot moving already, then adjust it, and tighten the bandage.
Then place the sick person's sleep on his back and put a small pillow under his chin near his sleep for the night, so that when he is awakened, help may be brought to him from his side. Therefore, dislocate the neck-fracture through elevation of the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck and do not loosen the bandage unless it happens at the place arising from itching or swelling, for up to twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see his face turning towards that [place]. And add some [material] in binding and release it until it is healed and hardened.
The dislocated neck is bound and supported for as many as 20 days, and sometimes it occurs in fewer than those [days].
Chapter on the repair of a fracture in the flat bone (spatula).
The Spatula seldom breaks, and at its broad place: and from it, only the edges break off.
When it breaks from there, and breaks within its midst, and is not recognized except by touch: then prepare for its treatment according to the figure of the break. Bring it back to its natural shape in every way possible that is within your ability.
Then place on it a mixture of milled powder with egg white and soft starch, and then cover with a downy cloth. Then place on top of it a wide star made from thin wood according to the size of the Spatula or slightly larger.
If there is a hollow under the star for any part of the Spatula, then fill the hollow with soft starch until the star sinks to the same level.
Then fix it on top with long alum thread, with suitable tightening and firmness of the star, until it cannot be moved from its place.
And examine the bandage every day. Whenever the bandage becomes soft, tighten it, and if the star moves from its place, let it lie weak on its healthy side.
And the Spatula is restored within twenty days or twenty-five days.
When this number is completed, then loosen the bandage: and you will be safe. For it is made of bones whose fracture is not feared, nor their solution.
If, however, a bone splinter comes out and pierces under the skin: then cut it off above. And do as we said for the break of a collarbone using a hot poultice, if something happens from it.
Chapter on the restoration of the broken chest.
Chest sometimes breaks in the middle, and it happens rarely. Its extremities break or are fractured more frequently due to their accidents.
When it breaks at its middle, which declines towards the lower parts, and this is accompanied by intense pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, and perhaps blood vomiting, and a hollow appears on the fractured bone, which is not concealed to the sense; the restoration is that the weak one lies down with his back upon it, and place between the splints a pillow.
Then press his humeri (shoulders), and bring the ribs from two sides or flanks with the hands; and chisel on its equal side, in any way possible that can be attained, until the figure of the bone returns, according to what is necessary.
Then place upon it an ointment and wool, and place above it a star from a thin board of elm or yew, or similar ones in lightness, after you fold it in a cloth. Then chisel on the binding (the splint) over the fractured bone to prevent it from being dislodged; and go with the binding according to its roundness to the back several times, and tighten it with a fitting ligature.
Then consider the binding at every hour, and whenever it becomes soft, tighten it; and if its necessity provokes its solution due to itching or corrosion that happens in the place, or pain, or abscess; then hurry, and dissolve it, remove the ointment, and correct whatever happened with the method of its cure.
Then bring back the ointment if you see it facing it (the fracture); and connect a ligature to it until it is healed.
Chapter on the restoration of ribs when they break.
You should know that the fracture of a costal bone does not happen except in thick places following the back, and from their extremities from the anterior part. And it does not happen to them by rubbing, unless because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of this does not escape the sense during examination with fingers.
And its restoration is done in such a way that it is leveled with the fingers according to the possible manner, until it becomes equal in form, as necessary. Then apply ointments, and bind the broken bone with a splint, if necessary.
And if the fracture of a costal bone is sloping towards the inside: then there occurs intense pain in the weak, and puncture, like the puncture that happens to one who has pleurisy; because the bone pierces the membrane, and also there occurs difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood much; and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients were skilled in this with many devices; and among them are those who say that you should put food for the weak that generates inflammation and wind, so that the stomach is inflamed and extended, and pushed the fracture towards the outside.
We, however, reject that a cause for making a warm abscess should be made if it does not occur. If, however, it has already occurred, then add this to it.
And some of them said: Place a bladder on the spot, then squeeze it with force, and it will be more similar to reasoning. However, it is feared that the bladder attracts excess to the spot, due to its weakness.
And some of them said: The place should be covered with wool that has been soaked in hot oil; and fleas should be placed between the ribs until it is filled; so that the binding will be equal when you roll it according to its roundness.
Then the sick person is treated for pleurisy with food and medicine.
If the patient is severely constricted and cannot bear it, and we pierce the skin with puncture causing pain, and we are afraid for the patient, then it is necessary that we cut over the area and expose the broken rib. Then place on it the instrument that conserves the sifac, whose memory has been mentioned earlier; and cut the bone easily, and cut the same (bone).
Then we will bring together two lips of the wound if it is large with stitching; and treat the same (wound) with plasters until it heals.
If, meanwhile, there occurs a hot abscess, then hurry and pour pulverized charcoal into rose oil, and place it on the site; and the sick person is treated with what suits an abscess.
Furthermore, a poultice should be applied to the side on which he lies until it heals.
Chapter on the restoration of fracture of the spine and neck.
Fractures of the neck bones sometimes occur, and rarely is there attrition for them; and similarly with the vertebrae of the back. When such a case happens to someone, and you want to know if it will heal or not, then investigate. For if you see that the person's hands are already softened, stunned and dead; and he cannot move them or extend them or contract them; and when you press them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, it feels nothing nor pain in them, then you know that it will not heal, and in many cases it is mortal.
If he moves them and feels the pressure with his fingers and pricking, then you know that the neck bone is healthy, and that the sick person will heal with treatment.
If it should happen with the spondylus (vertebra) of the back similar to that one, and you wish to know whether it can be healed or not: then consider his feet; then if you see that they are softened, and the same thing happens in them as happens in hands: next, when it lies on its back, it expels wind and vomiting involuntarily: and when it lies on its belly, it expels vomiting involuntarily: and when it lies on its back and wishes to have intercourse but cannot: then you will know that it is fatal. Do not exert efforts in treating it (the vertebra or spondylus).
If nothing of that happens to him, the case is lighter.
And the treatment for that which happens to him (the vertebra or spondylus) is as follows: you should seek a hot lancing (or cauterization): thus, you place on the Spondylus (vertebra) a rubbed oil of roses only; or with saturated eggs of a sheep.
Place it (the hot lancing or cauterization) several times a day until the warm abscess subsides: then place on one location of a strengthening and drying plaster and bind it tightly over it with a bandage; and instruct the patient to rest quietly and calmly. He should not sleep on the part where he found pain until it is healed.
If it happens during the rubbing of the satheus bone or if something separated from it occurs: then you must remove it and let it heal over the skin. Next, apply two lips of the wound (if it is large), with stitching. Then care for him with some ointment until he is healed.
If the last bone of the alhausos (spine or vertebra) breaks and it is the end of the tail: then you must insert your left thumb into the anus, and with the other hand, you should align the broken bone according to what is possible, and secondly in accordance with the alignment.
Then place it upon him, and remove it; and care for the wound with the treatment that preceded, until it heals.
On the Restoration of Anus.
Chapter XI.
Rarely do the bones of the anus break. If they break, then the fracture of these bones is not complete, but rather a comminution at their extremities; and they are split lengthwise and turned towards the interior; and there occurs pain and swelling in the place, and puncturing; and the limb that breaks becomes numb.
And its restoration is to extend your hand over it until you feel the exact shape of the fracture.
If the fracture is only at its extremities, then align that fracture according to your preparation, until it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is along its length or has already turned towards the inside; then make the weak person lie on his belly until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When you have aligned it (the fracture), place an ointment on it; then place a splint made of wood or hide over it, and tie a binding around it that will not cause the fracture to shift or the splint to be removed.
And align the concavity of these (the fractured bones) with the one that fills it, until the binding takes hold of it according to its alignment; and tell the weak person to sleep on his back or on his healthy side.
If a hot abscess occurs (on the anus); then cease extending it (the fracture) and its restoration until the hot abscess subsides; and place whatever it lies on, according to what preceded, and then return to its restoration; and bind it as needed.
If it happens to a bone that it becomes fractured, extract it.
On the Restoration of a Fracture of the Thighbone.
The aid is that which is between the elbow and the head of the Spatula.
If it breaks, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of them is: that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, in this shape which follows.
And bind on both ends two thongs.
Then it is suspended from a raised place, and the sick one sits upon a chair. Then cast his broken arm upon a stick, until it reaches the joint of that stick, where its splinter is attached to the wood.
Then place something heavy above it, so that the staff will extend towards lower parts. Then the physician shall press the fracture with both hands until it returns to its proper place.
And another method is that the weak one should lie on his neck, and hang his hand from his neck with a strap. Then order two attendants to hold the part above the fracture with their hands: and let one of them hold the lower part, and each shall stretch his own in that direction.
And if you wish for a stronger extension, then tie a bandage below the fracture and above it with another: and let each of the two attendants stretch their respective parts in opposite directions.
If the fracture is near to the end of the shoulder, then it is necessary to place one bandage below the joint, and another one towards the elbow under the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is near to the elbow: then it is necessary to place a bandage on that place and another one above the elbow.
Then equal the fracture easily without effort, until it aligns as necessary, and is applied properly: then bind it if no hot abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then release the binding until the seventh day. Place linen cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil on it until the abscess settles down: then bind it; and the method of binding is to apply a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a new cloth layer over the plaster. Then bring the arm and place his hand on the support, and put his open hand on his shoulder.
Place a cloth and bind it over the assisting arm, so that the arm remains in place like a star; if it does not prevent anything from being removed, nor be altered by something broken bone.
If you fear that something may be altered by the broken bone, then use a larger and stronger star (astellus). It is so that you place a larger and stronger star over the fracture itself. Place between every star the width of a finger; and let the length of the stars be according to the fracture, with an addition of three fingers from all sides.
Then bind over the stars as I said at the beginning of this chapter.
It is so that your bandage be tighter over the place of the fracture. And as the fracture extends more, the binding should be less tight.
If you see the position of the stars and the bandage, as we said, at the hour when you restore the member, then do so; and if you fear a hot abscess, then release the binding and the stars until the seventh day, as we said.
Then consider the bandage for three days, so that itchiness, swelling or food does not reach the member due to the tightness of the binding. Correct this entire thing as I said.
But if you are confident that nothing will happen from it; then do not loosen the bandage, unless after three days or more.
Let the sick person lie on his back and place his hand on his stomach; and put a full wool blanket under the assisting arm equally.
Consider at every hour of the night or day that the shape of the broken member is not loosened nor is the binding made softer. Correct this entire thing with your own strength.
Place the food of the sick according to the order we have prescribed, so that it be a subtle food initially, until the bone begins to bind; then it is necessary to increase his food.
For the custom of helping and healing wounds is to be bound for forty days; therefore, when it has become bound, it is necessary to loosen it and administer a bath, and treat it with ointments that correct it.
If the fracture is foul, scrape it; then do not loosen the bandage and nails from it for fifty days, nor until two months have passed.
Chapter on the restoration of a broken arm.
The arm is made up of two bones, which are called the two forearm bones.
One of them is small; and that one follows the thumb. And the other is large, and that one is positioned below the small one from its lower part.
Therefore, perhaps only the larger forearm bone is broken, or perhaps both are broken.
When the smaller forearm bone is broken at the top, then the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the larger forearm bone is broken from below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And it becomes worse if both bones are broken at once.
If the bone that is broken is the smaller forearm bone at the top, then it is appropriate for the doctor to be present during its restoration, so that he may apply a small extension easily until it equalizes itself.
If however the larger forearm bone is the one that is broken: then a strong traction must be applied.
And it is necessary to arrange the hand during its restoration and extend its extension over the pulley. The thumb of the hand should be higher than all the fingers; and the inferior joint should be higher than all the fingers, or the remaining ones. A weak person sitting should have a square shape on himself, and the pulley should be before him, or directly in front of him, at a height, so that the weak man does not exert himself too much.
Then let the attendant extend the arm from its lower part, either with his hand or with a rope. And another assistant should extend it likewise from above.
Then the physician aligns the bone until he brings it back to its best possible shape.
If there are fragments of a broken bone, then try to put all the pieces back into their place by your own effort.
If it appears that a fragment of bone is separated and pierces the skin, and there is no hope for its restoration: then fill in the hole over it, and remove it as we have said in what has been mentioned before.
If there is a fracture with a wound, I have already written a special chapter about it, so take care of that from here onward.
If a hot abscess should happen at the beginning of your treatment, then fold a cloth with wax made from rose oil, and white beeswax, and let it be neither too thick nor too thin. Tie the cloth over it with a fine ligature until the abscess subsides. Then remove the wax, and place prepared plaster made from flour mill dust and egg white. Then place a splint (or wooden support), and the splint that is placed on the fracture should be slightly wider and stronger than the fracture itself.
And know that the number of splints for an arm is six in most arrangements; whether it is a fracture in one splint or in two splints at once.
Then place your ligature at the stronger and more intense part of the fracture and somewhat further towards the upper or lower parts of it; or place a softer ligature, according to what was previously remembered about it at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the bandages surrounding the fracture be made of soft, moist cloth; and they should not be hard or rough. And let the thread used for binding be medium-thickness linen as we have said.
Examine the limb and ligament after days. If something occurs which requires correction, such as itching of the limb: then it is necessary to wash the affected limb with warm water until the itching subsides and let the limb rest without constriction until it has settled; then tie again.
If the ligature has already been softened and the bone removed, and similar things: then correct it all with your skill and examine again.
For if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive constriction of the ligature, then it is necessary to soften it somewhat and let it rest for some days until it reaches the nourishment again; then tie it up again.
But if nothing from those mentioned above occurs to the sick man, then it is not necessary to untie it unless after twenty days or about that.
Then the hand of the sick man is suspended at his neck. And let his neck be kept equal and protected from excessive movements. Make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is healed within thirty or forty days; it may be healed within twenty-eight days. Everything depends on the dispositions of the temperaments and the dispositions of virtue.
On the restoration of hand fractures.
Know that the scissors-hand and finger joints rarely break. It does not happen to him except from repeated friction.
When the foot fracture or joint occurs from friction: then it is necessary that the weak quadrate sits: and before him there should be a seat according to equality. Then place his hand upon it extended.
The assistant shall spread out the broken bones, and the physician shall equalize them until they are joined together by a good union.
Then it is necessary to place plaster and wool, unless there occurs a hot abscess. Then place the splint on top according to the size of the place; and you shall now roll it up in a linen cloth.
If the fracture is around the toes approximately the navel of the foot, then make something similar to a ball from cloth; and command the patient to bend over it with his injured foot; then it shall be bound with a long cloth.
And let the splint be made of leather, in which there is softness, so that the skin of the sole may be applied to it; and bind it as necessary.
If the fracture is on the outside, then it is necessary to place one splint on top and another below on the foot; and keep the hand open and straight. Then place a bandage, as the hand is rolled up; and extend it between the fingers with a ligament.
If there occurs a fracture in any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it shall be equalized as necessary. Then bind it with the foot; and if there are more than one, place a straight splint under it, smaller; so that the fracture may be raised and not moved.
If the fracture is in the remaining fingers such as the middle finger, the index finger, the little finger or the ring finger, then equalize it and bind it with a healthy finger that follows it; or bind all of them according to continuity; and it is better. Or place a smaller splint over it, as we said for the thumb.
And be careful in your restoration, and after this position of hot abscesses; and apply to it as necessary, whenever something occurs from it, according to its description.
Restoration of a Hip Fracture.
The thighbone (OS Coxae) is often broken and palpable to the touch because it turns towards the inner and posterior parts.
And its restoration is done by tightening a ligament above the fracture, and another ligament below the fracture. The patient is laid supine on his back.
Then, each ligament used for the treatment should be extended to its respective side equally: and this is when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If the fracture is close to the root of the thighbone (Coxae), then let a ligament fall on one side, so that the extension goes upwards; and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is close to the knee, then let a ligament be near the knee, so that the extension goes downwards.
The doctor then sets the bone with both his hands until he brings it back to its original shape; and joins the bone by a good union.
Then, one must apply an ointment and a bandage if no abscess occurs in the limb. If an abscess occurs in this, then let it be for several days until the abscess subsides and becomes warm; then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be like this. It is necessary to wrap a hard cloth around the fracture, that is, binding; and double it twice or three times; and there should be an excess left over from it. Then wrap the leg until it reaches the heel at the root of the toes.
And insert a long thread between the thighbone and the leg near the knee towards the lower part; and the ends of the thread should come from the upper part of both sides. Then wrap again over the leg and the thighbone where there is an excess of cloth from elsewhere.
Then place stars (or pins) on the thigh at the location of the fracture and place one star over the limb's bone.
Then fill the voids between the thigh and the hip with soft cloth to make it equal (in length).
Then begin the strong incision from the middle above the location of the fracture with three or four revolutions. And as you extend the ligament from the location of the fracture, let your incision be shorter and softer, and more gentle.
Then aim for the two ends of the cord that you inserted between the thigh and the hip. Tie three stars (or pins) to each end. Then go with the two ends of the cord to the lower parts until you reach the ankle bone of the foot. And bind the other ends of the stars as well to prevent the cord from moving away from its place.
Then release (the pressure) on the incision as long as it remains, so that it does not cause rot to the limb, or abscess, or swelling, and similar things. If anything happens from this. Approach then with the solution of it and correct everything that happened there, according to what we have said many times.
But if the broken bone splinter pierces the other bone: then it is necessary to equalize (the fracture) if it is possible for you. And if not, fill it up and remove it; take care of the one whose memory preceded until it heals.
Sometimes this thigh fracture is bound (immobilized) beyond what the pins added for it, as we said in aid and arm.
However, in repairing this kind of fracture, there is no limp in the lame person. But if it is repaired alone, without applying the other leg to it, then it is necessary that the lame person always limps.
And know that the hip (coxa) is bound for fifty days, or it may be slightly increased or slightly decreased: all of this depends on the diversity of temperaments and other dispositions.
On the restoration of a fracture of the knee joint (Rotulae).
Know that a fracture of the knee joint (Rotulae) rarely occurs, but it often happens that it is rubbed.
But if a fracture occurs, then it will only be either a crack or a hole in its parts. And this is with an injury and without an injury. It stands on it (the fractured bone) entirely with sensation.
And the restoration of this is such that it is made equal to what was broken from its parts with fingers, until it is joined, and united according to what is possible by equality and ease, and construction (reconstruction).
Then apply a plaster made from wheat flour or milled flour with egg white: and place on top of it a round stopper (if necessary): and bind above it with a band suitable for that.
Then consider all the dispositions that we have mentioned in other fractures: such as a warm abscess and similar to it: so that you counteract every occurrence with what is suitable for it until it heals.
On the restoration of a fracture of the leg (Cruris).
Know that the leg (Cruris) consists of two bones, one of which is large and called by the name Cruris; and the other is small and called Focile.
And it happens that there are also fractures of both the leg bones, as with the two bones of the arm; and because of this, the restoration of these is equal to the restoration of the arm, and the procedure is one.
If both bones break at once, the leg (Crus) becomes deformed in all its parts.
If a bone is fractured more finely, it turns to the front part.
If a bone is fractured coarsely, you will find it below; it belongs to those that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, equalization, and ligature of stars equally according to it.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a dirty fracture with many set-backs, it should have a smaller extension and softer alluvial treatment by you.
Moreover, in the leg there is something from the operation that is similar to the arm; and it is that when you equalize stars, and complete your entire operation, then take two bands made of pine wood, which are used in the making of roof tiles placed under tables; they are made from wooden spatulas; choose those with small thickness, and neither too small nor too large; and their length should be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then fold over each cloth with two folds according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side and the other part on the opposite side; they should be from the knee down to the foot.
Then bind two bands at three places from both ends and in the middle. For when ligature is forbidden, the leg inclines to the right and left, and it stands steadily on a good stance.
And now, a splint made of wood according to the length of the leg should be administered, and the leg should be placed in it to keep it still.
It is especially important that you do this when there is a wound properly with the fracture.
Then consider the leg every day and study it diligently from swelling, edema, or other problems. If something happens among those, then oppose it with what is necessary until it heals, if God wills it.
On the Fracture of Bones in the Foot, and Fingers.
Calcaneum certainly does not undergo a complete fracture.
The bones of the foot sometimes undergo a fracture, and it happens seldom to the fingers; but they do not break except from repeated rubbing in various positions.
If a fracture of the bones of the foot occurs, and you see those bones rise up against each other, then place the sick person's foot on the ground prepared as when walking.
Then stand you there, and place your foot on what is raised by those bones; afterwards press or tread it down: and hold it even, until it returns to its places.
Then place an ointment and a plaster on it; and place under the heel of the foot a small board with two flat heads.
Then tie it with a suitable bandage when you have rolled it up with cloths and the other things that are needed.
When three or four days have passed, then unbind the bandage. For you will find the bones aligned if they are fractured or dislocated. Therefore, the operation is as you see.
If some fingers are broken, then restore them, and align them as I told you in the treatment of the fingers of the hand. Then place a peg under the finger according to its length and let it be slightly wider; then place the board that I told you about under the foot and bind it with a suitable bandage.
If what is broken consists of two or more bones, then place a peg of the appropriate size under each finger, rolled up in linen cloth; and bind the board at the bottom of the foot; let the board stand on one head of the board outside the heel to hold it firmly.
It is fitting for you to remember not to forget all that I have said to you regarding other fractures, concerning the accidents which were mentioned; and apply this to every accident as needed.
On the fracture of a woman's jaw, and the clavicle, and the man's penis.
When a woman's jaw is fractured, then make her sit square-like (in a reclined position). Then incline her towards the back of the same (head) somewhat, and hold her backwards.
Then fill the woman's jaw with a linen wrap until it is full; and let it be shaped on the jaw like a sphere (rounded).
Then strike the woman gently, and lift her back gradually. For this linen wrap will come out before the opening of the jaw becomes like a sphere, and the broken bone returns to its place.
Then place a feather upon her back. When he (the physician) wants to insert his hand, let him remove the linen wrap easily, then insert; and return it in the same manner as when he first inserted it; then return to the feather first.
He should do this for seven days or about, for the bone itself is restored.
And if you wish, take the bladder of an ox and fill it with a reed tube, and insert the entire bladder into her jaw. Then blow through the reed tube forcefully until the bladder inflates inside the jaw, for the fracture returns to its place.
Then it is filled with linen wrap and remains for some days as we have said, until it heals.
When the clavicle of a man or woman is fractured, then administer its restoration and use what we have said regarding the equalization for the bone of Ancha.
And the rectum is not hidden in those fractures which are close, which rarely happen, especially in one who has some inclination or practice.
And understand my book very much, for the fracture itself signifies to you in many dispositions a straight path for its restoration and for its constriction; therefore understand it.
When the penis of a man is broken, take the neck of a goose and insert the rod into it. Then place upon him a cover made of cloth, wrap it up, and let it be for three days or around those days, and it will heal if God wishes.
On the restoration of a broken bone, when it is with a wound.
It is fitting for the one who has a broken bone with a wound, especially if it is large like the hip or scapula or something similar, that you hurry and apply the phlebotomist; if they inform you of the conditions of phlebotomy as we have mentioned before.
If the wound flows blood, then it is necessary that you hurry to the excision of it, so that you sprinkle on top of it a powder made from crushed chalk; if there is no other present to you.
Then begin the restoration of the fracture on that same day and do not delay it unless an abscess occurs.
If however a hot abscess occurs, then delay the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the abscess settles down. And do not approach him on the third and fourth day at all, for you would make bad things happen to him.
If the broken bone is projecting above the skin and detected: then it is necessary to compress it, and bring it back with your hands with ease and slight extension.
If however the extension is not prepared, and its equal with your hands: then reduce it with this instrument. And is the instrument which is made of iron, whose length is the quantity of seven or eight fingers, and its width according to the size of the wound. And for this reason it is necessary for the physician to take from them three or four according to the size which he needs for the cure of any kind of fracture.
And let it be round: and let there be a small thickness within it so that it is not doubled during the compression upon it during the hour of the operation; and let it have sharp edges, with a border at its ends. And let the thickness of it be from the middle of it to the lower part.
And this is its shape.
In this operation it is necessary to place the sharp edge of it concave against the end of the projecting bone; and push it with one blow until the bone returns and becomes equal in some way.
Therefore try to make the ends of the fracture opposite each other.
If the end of the fracture is fine and does not accept the instrument well, then it is necessary to cut the edge of that bone until the instrument has control over it.
But if you cannot reduce them with what I have mentioned, then cut it with the saws which I have said; or cut it with one of the saws in any way that is possible for you.
Then plane off what remains in the bone due to roughness and small layers.
When the bone is reduced and finds that there is a weak spot after its reduction causing a violent pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Make it so. Thou conferrest a great remedy upon the sick one, when thou complete the restoration which thou dost make of the bone.
Then immerse the cloth in wine of stibium, black. And properly if it is in summer. And place not thereon greenish substances, nor anything containing oil; so that putrefaction and corruption may not occur in it.
Then administer astelas at the hour of completion of thy restoration of the bone. And leave the wound exposed; thus, cut with forceps the hole in the fracture according to the size of the wound.
And take care not to suture the wound with the fracture. For this have many foolish physicians often done it; and they have caused death upon their sick ones, or corruption, or gangrene, that is, pus. And let thy suture be soft, gentle, and diverse from the sutures of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or if it is a large wound, and thou fearest some evil incidents which we have mentioned, and thou findest pain in a suspended place; then it is not necessary that thou place astelas upon it. And make for them immobilizations with hard cloths in the place of astelas, and suture it with them.
And when it is a day or two after, and thou seest that the wound already begins to produce pus; then take away the cloth which thou hast placed upon it with wine. Then administer linen and ointments with which we are accustomed to treat wounds; such as ointment of four ingredients (tetrapharmacon), and similar things.
And it suits thee, that thou loosen the bandage. Consider the wound every day, both morning and evening, until it consolidates, and heals.
It is necessary that the limb should be prepared so that the pus runs out from it easily towards the lower parts.
If many days pass without the wound healing and the pus is not discharged from it: then you know that there are small splinters of bone there. Therefore, one must examine the wound with a probe. How many of those splinters are separated, remove them and extract them. And those which are not separated and pierce the limb, causing pain, try to cut them and remove them in any way that is possible for you.
If a blister or boil should happen on the wound, that is to say if there are symptoms of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary that you apply to each one of them what is suitable for it from the remedies mentioned in its own chapter.
And in that which my speech should remain, and for whom you make your mind present when a bone is broken and protrudes on the limb, such as the hip bone or shoulder bones and similar large bones, it is not to presume the attraction of it, for often death occurs from this, indeed, leave it alone until it putrefies.
Perhaps it will fall off by itself within twenty or thirty days.
Therefore, treat the wound if you see fit to do so; and if not, leave it alone.
Chapter on the treatment of a nodule at the site of a fracture.
This nodule often happens in the site of healing of a fracture. It is particularly those near the joints that are corrupted and form an abscess on the limb. And perhaps it prevents the limb from performing its natural function.
Consider then, if the bandage is recent: administer medications that have astringent properties, such as aloe, olibanum, myrrh, sarcocolla, acacia, and similar ones.
Take some or all of these [medicines]: mix them with astringent wine, or egg white, or vinegar; and place them on the bandage on top of a wad, and tightly bind it over the affected area.
And release the binding, do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and repeat the process until the bandage is removed.
Or tighten it well over the lead plate. For lead has the property of removing anything that is feared from the body.
If the bandage has already turned into a stone and is tightened and urgently requires removal: then bind over it from the upper part of it, and cut off any excessive growth; or grind it with certain scrapers until it is removed; and care for it until it heals.
Section on treatment when the limb restores itself, but remains weak after the injury, not according to its natural state.
When a bone fracture heals and the limb remains weak afterwards, this is not done unless for many reasons. Among them is an excess of loosening and binding of the ligament not as required: either due to redundancy of tightening of the ligaments until nutriment is forbidden to flow to the member: or due to excessive embrocation in a non-appropriate time: or due to scarcity of blood in the body of the sick and its weakness.
And the treatment for this is, to feed the sick, and make his body tender until blood multiplies within him. And administer a bath. And pour joy and happiness over him.
Then place pitch on the member, so that the pitch attracts much nourishment towards it. And constantly embrocate his member with warm water until the nourishment runs, and returns to its natural form.
Chapter on the treatment of broken bones when they are restored twisted.
When the member which has already been restored after healing is found twisted or protruding of the broken bone, or swollen: and it defaces the form of the limb, but the limb is not prohibited from its natural operation: then one should not receive the opinion of him who thinks that the limb should be broken again.
And there have been many foolish doctors and restorers who did this in our land.
This operation is indeed very condemnable, leading to death or great fear that it may be fatal.
But if the limping and swelling are recent: then it is necessary to embrocate with warm water in which have been boiled softening herbs such as althea leaves and its root, melilotus, and similar ones. And make for him a plaster with a softening plaster like well-prepared dyachilon.
Take a mucilage from the root of the althea, grind it with chicken fat or sesame oil; and apply it with that.
Take figs (that are) fat, grind them with pigeon dung and similar substances that are named for reducing consolidation.
And sometimes a knot is loosened with persistent friction, an old and gentle method which is done with moist hands. Let movement be administered to the limb for every hour.
If however the crookedness has already occurred and it is strong and it has been turned into a stone, and its necessity for treatment with iron has arisen: then it is necessary that it be cut above it, and the continuation of the bone be loosened, and what is superfluous from the knot or the bone with finer chisels be cut away; and let ease be administered to it with diligence and care.
Chapter on Dislocation.
Dislocation is the coming out of some joints from their place. Why it should be prohibited from movement, and become deformed in the form of the limb. And this causes pain to occur on the weak side, and very severe injuries.
When therefore a dislocation occurs, then it is necessary to hasten to its restoration; nor should one delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place will relax, and perhaps with it the reduction of the dislocation.
Therefore it should not be moved nor extended during the hour of relaxation. Because many times it causes a spasm, and painful injuries to occur.
But when this happens, then it is necessary that you hurry to the bloodletting of the weak person. Then it should be left until the relaxation relaxes.
Then anoint the limb with hot water and oil. Then bring back easily (or gently).
And rub all the limbs with it, when memory comes to its place.
I myself have already arranged the chapters on dislocation, even according to what came before in fracture, from the upper part of the body downwards to its lower part.
Chapter on the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two Jaws scarcely dislocate except rarely.
And dislocation occurs according to two modes. For it is either moved away from its place by slight movement and becomes slightly softened. The dislocation should be reduced by complete reduction, until it becomes soft to the chest part, until the saliva of the weak runs and it cannot hold it, nor can it close its mouth, and its tongue is rolled up with speech.
When the dislocation is small, then by itself it returns through various dispositions with a few.
If the dislocation is complete and whole: then it must be administered a quick reduction, and it should not be delayed much; and it is necessary that the assistant holds the head of the weak; and the doctor inserts one finger of his hand into the root of the Mandible dislocation, if the dislocation is from one part. Or he inserts two fingers at the same time, if the dislocation is from two parts. And the remaining fingers of his hand should be outside, which are equal.
And command the weak, that he cleans the Mandibles of himself, and purifies them for eating to every part. And the doctor presses the Mandible, and pushes it until it returns to its place.
If the reduction is difficult, especially if both Mandibles are at the same time: then administer steam with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy; nor delay the reduction of both entirely, as we have said.
When they return and meet, and the mouth of the weak is closed, and they are not softened: then place poultices on them made of pieces of cloth with wax, and rose oil.
Then it is tied with soft bands.
And let the sleep of the weak be on his back; and let his head be standing between two poultices, so that it does not move to the right and left; nor let him try or be forced to chew something; instead let food be placed before him gently softened, until when the pains are removed, and the Mandible is loosened: then he eats what he wants, and that is given with ease.
Do not work on his mouth's operation for eating and drinking, and for talking, until the mandible has healed and is well.
If the reduction of the two mandibles becomes difficult when they are dislocated at any time and do not return to their places: then often fevers occur, and continuous diarrhea; and perhaps the weak person's abdomen ruptures, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
Therefore, when you see that: then you will know, it is a loss, and often he who experiences this dies within ten days.
Chapter on the reduction of dislocations in the neck (collarbone) and the end of the upper arm.
The collarbone is not dislocated from the inside on the side due to its connection with the chest. It becomes dislocated occasionally towards the outside, and that is noticeable to sensation.
The restoration of it is that the sick one lies down on his back, and spreads out his arms. Then bring your hand close to the place with strong compaction, because it will return.
Then put on top of it an ointment and a linen cloth, and tie it.
However, the ends that follow the elbow should not be dislocated unless rarely. If it is dislocated on some day: then it must be returned and equalized according to what we have said, and that which is prepared for you.
Then put on top of it an ointment and a linen cloth, and tie it with a bandage. And command the sick man to remain in calmness and stillness until he is healed.
And when this same treatment returns the arm's end, even if it has separated from its place.
Chapter on the Reduction of Dislocation of the Arm.
Know that the shoulder does not dislocate except in three ways.
One is when it dislocates towards the lower part, towards the armpit. The second is when it dislocates towards the chest. And perhaps it dislocates towards the upper part of the shoulder, and that happens rarely.
Nor is it dislocated to the backside, because of the shoulder blade. Nor is it dislocated to the front side, because of the nerves.
And indeed it is most often dislocated and exits towards the lower part turned towards the armpit; especially in those whose flesh is less. Because it quickly comes out of them and quickly goes back in.
In those whose flesh is numerous, it is contrary to that one, namely because it exits with difficulty, and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some men with a blow or a fall and the humerus is dislocated and hotly cauterized, and it is believed because it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary that you try that, until you are certain of its nature, and then proceed with its cure.
A dislocation is known when it is turned towards the little finger: and distinguish between a dislocated humerus, and a healthy humerus. For you will find among them a manifest difference, and in the head of the Humerus you will find a concavity, and under the little finger by touch the head of the humerus seems like an egg: and the weak cannot bring the hand to the ear, nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if dislocated towards the chest or upwards: then you will find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
The reduction of this kind of dislocation is easy when it is recent, or if the child is weak.
And its reduction is that the assistant raises his hand towards the upper part. Then place your thumbs under the little finger of it, and lift the joint with force towards its place; and the assistant raises his hand, and extends it upwards. Then he puts it back down, for it quickly returns to its place.
If it does not return through what I have said, and from the beginning when the dislocation occurred many days have passed: then it is necessary that the weak person be bathed in warm water, and ointment administered which softens and soothes, such as if they boil the roots of althea, fenugreek, and melilotus in water, and apply it.
Then the sick person lies down on his back: and a sphere and wool should be placed under his navel, which is equal in size between softness and tightness.
Then the doctor places his heel on the sphere, and he lifts up the head of the arm with force, and pulls the hand of the sick person downwards.
And if you wish, bring him back in this manner: and it is, so that you make the present man who is taller than the sick person stand on his side, and he will insert his arm under the navel of the sick person: and lift up the navel of his upwards, until the sick person is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the hand of the sick person downwards to his stomach. If the sick person is lightweight: then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, so as to weigh him down; for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also brought back in another manner: and it is, so that a long piece of wood is formed on the ground, whose head is rounded like a cuff; as a mace handle; which should not be too heavy, nor too delicate. Then place something soft under the sick person when they are placed on the log; and the sick person should stand along the length of the log.
Then his hand is stretched downwards on one side, and his body on the other side with force; for the dislocation returns to its place quickly.
If the reduction is difficult by all that has been said, then use this method: and it is, so that you take a piece of wood whose length is the measurement of two forearms, and its width four fingers, and its thickness two fingers; and its head should be rounded, to alleviate the entrance into the deep navel, in accordance with this shape.
Then, soft cloths are to be tied over the round head, so as not to injure the weak wood [referring to a part of a surgical instrument].
Then, place it under the weak spot of the patient, so that the hand or arm can be extended over the wood until below: and tie the wooden support to the arm and the end of the hand.
Then, place the arm on the step of the ladder at its width, and extend the hand below, and let the rest of the body hang suspended from the other side. The joint is to enter immediately.
Once the entrance is completed in whatever way possible: then it is necessary to place beneath the weak spot a sphere of equal size made from wool. Then place the prepared plaster made of milled powder with oil of myrrh and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it is turned [over].
Then bind under the weak spot a suitable stitch: and turn the bandage over the plaster above: and connect the hand to its own neck, and let it not be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary to place a sparse food until the member recovers strength, because it is quicker for its healing.
Then, after seven or five days, let it be removed with movement. If it hardens and does not soften, then it is healed.
If the joint is dislocated many times due to moisture that happens to it or due to another cause: then it is necessary to administer cautery with three holes, according to what was stated in the chapter on cauterization.
If all of this is done, and the bandage is loosened after seven days, and the joint does not harden, and the plaster and stitch are repeatedly placed on it but do not firm up, and it falls, and cannot be raised to higher places: then you will know that the nerves that are toward the head of the shoulder have been cut or stretched or softened: and you will know that the joint will never be able to heal in its place.
Dislocations that occur towards the chest and breast, or are reduced to the rear, are done with pressure and extension using hands: until it returns, and further compression and care are administered in it, until it heals.
If there is heaviness in the member after healing and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use baths often, until that heaviness lessens, and it returns to its original state.
Chapter on the cure of shoulder dislocations.
Know that the union of the shoulder dislocates with difficulty, and it returns with difficulty as well; and indeed, it is dislocated in all directions, but particularly towards the front or rear.
And its dislocation will not be hidden from you: because it falls under sight and touch, according to whatever form it is dislocated. And when you join a dislocated shoulder with a healthy one, the condition will be clearly shown to you. For the joint should be hollowed out, and it cannot bend the arm nor touch its own humerus with it.
And you must hasten and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot abscess occurs to it.
For if a hot abscess occurs to it, the reduction becomes difficult; and perhaps it will not heal at all, especially when it is towards the rear. For it is worse than all kinds of dislocations, and of more violent pain; and death often accompanies it many times.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is as follows: extend the hand with both hands; let the arm be extended, and the doctor's hands be above and under the shoulder, and he himself pushes the joint with the tips of his fingers or with the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
If it is a dislocation to the front: then [the hand] returns so that it doubles with its own hand, until it strikes with the root of its palm the elbow which is dislocated.
If the dislocation does not obey in reduction, then administer strong and violent extension; it is such that two servants extend the arm; and the same two servants hold it so that it is not moved during the extension.
Then the arm is turned to every part after it has been wrapped over its own hand with a long cloth or a wide bandage.
And when the doctor touches with his hand the joint, he binds it with oil; so that it is one of those things which help for lubricity of the joint with ease. Then he pushes the joint with a strong push until it returns.
After its reduction, it is necessary to place over it a plaster containing styptics and extract with egg white; and it is tied with a suitable ligature. The arm is suspended from the neck of the sick person, and leave for some days.
Then it is loosened. If the joint hardens in its place, then loose the bandage from it, and leave it.
And if you see that the joint is not bound well, then renew the plaster, and the bandage, and leave it for some days until it hardens. Then loosen it.
If there is pain after the reduction of the joint and it moves badly: then administer humectation in bathing, and subtle friction, and compression until it settles, or place over the joint of the leg, that is, the woolly fat of a ram. Then bind it and leave it over it for a day and night. Then remove it, and immerse it in the bath. When it sweats, then press the joint with moderate rubbing. Then repeat over the same spot three times with a pounce and secondly, with the introduction of bath, until it becomes softened.
And if you wish, place upon it dung of cow, moistened and heated with butter, and press upon it.
Make that [process] several times. It becomes softened, and returns to its own condition.
Chapter on the treatment of dislocations by Albucasis.
Mahasem's hand is frequently dislocated, that is, the joint between arm and hand.
The reduction of this dislocation is easy. On the contrary, other joints [are more difficult]. However, speed should be present in the reduction of this dislocation within the hour when it is dislocated, before the location is loosened; otherwise a hot abscess may occur there.
And the reduction of this dislocation is that Mahasem's weak hand should be placed on a table, and the servant should extend it; and the doctor should place his palm over the prominent part of the joint, and press on it until it returns.
However, if the palm has already retreated to the back of the hand, then the weak hand should be placed on a table for extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding towards the back of the hand, then the position of the hand should be internally over the table, that is, the inside part of the hand should be over the table so that the doctor's hand falls upon the prominent part of the joint.
If it returns immediately, that is good; and if not, then press it with a plaster to suppress the abscess; and leave it alone, do not repeat the process.
For if it does not tolerate the reduction process and more days pass without improvement, yet the joint remains in its twisted state; and nothing harms the weak hand except that it becomes softened, and cannot be pressed; then you will know that the nerves have been severed or rubbed.
The skill in that (situation) is not to tie it with a cautery, except for saving its life. And perhaps it contributes nothing.
Since Almahassem did not return, then place on him the plaster that I mentioned earlier. Then it should be tied and let go for five days; afterwards it should be untied and let go, and the hand should be exercised.
If its movement is hindered and something heavy occurs to it: then soften it with warm water and rub it gently several times until it becomes soft.
Chapter on the cure of finger dislocations.
When a finger is dislocated in any direction, or when the finger is dislocated to the back of the palm, or the palm's ventrum: then extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then hold the finger's head and hang it in the direction to which it is dislocated, and let it be for two days.
Then hang it and stretch it until it becomes straight on that same day.
And when it is night, tie it as mentioned; do not stop untying it during the day; exercise it with movement and tie it at night.
Do this for several days until it becomes tied (or immobilized).
And likewise do this if it (the finger) is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; tie it to that same part and perform your first treatment on it until it heals.
And likewise do this when it (the finger) is dislocated in other directions.
Chapter on the cure of spine dislocations (or vertebrae dislocations).
When someone has a spine (vertebra) dislocation of the back or neck, complete dislocation, or when many vertebrae are displaced from their places: then there is no cure for it, since death approaches the weak.
The sign of it is that the illness of a person leaves without will, nor can it be held; and many times certain members of his are softened from it, or two legs of his, or two arms of his, or one of them; or a vertebra is removed from its place.
Sometimes it is removed (of certain members), which often occurs in their removal from four parts.
Therefore, that which is removed towards the rear, is called Gibbosity.
The cure of it is to consider whether Gibbosity occurs from childhood; then there is no cure in it, nor healing at all.
But in that which occurs from an accident, or a blow, or similar things, the ancient ways of treatment have been said with a long speech. It rarely returns (when it is removed) with good result.
I myself have already abridged it from that (treatise), which explains very little of its substance due to my proximity to the intention; and in my explanation, I explained that and shaped its instrument differently from how they showed and explained it.
Therefore, in Gibbosity which occurs from the front side, there is no skill, nor healing from that.
And similarly in that which happens on both sides.
And it is not cured from them, except that which occurs on the back itself, as I am going to say; and it is necessary for the sick person to extend himself over his face on a plank equal to nearly a wall; and a damp cloth should be spread under him so as not to injure his chest.
Then a straight piece of wood is placed fixed in the mortar on the ground at the end of the plank, about his head; and another piece of wood about his feet at the end of the other plank; and let the assistant hold the wood, and it should not be fastened in the mortar. The other servant holds the other one.
Then, lean over the head of the sick person, and beneath his chin, tie a cord firmly: and extend the end of the cord to the wooden post that is near his head: and tie it there.
Then, tie another cord above his ankles, and above his knees, and at his fetters. Then gather all the cords together, and tie them to another wooden post that is near his feet.
Then let all the servants stretch out the cord to any part of the wooden post, and neither of the two posts should be moved from their places where they are fixed: but they should not be fastened, as we have said.
And the physician shall place his hands on the patient's shinbone firmly until he comes back; or he can place it on a board. Then he ties his feet to the board until he returns.
If he does not return with this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arms. And hollow out a hole in the wall as we said near the sick person, into which the end of the board enters. Then place the middle part of the board on the shinbone. And let the physician put his feet on the other end. And tie it tightly until the shinbone is bent and he returns to his place.
Then, if you wish, work with the lathe that is rotated by hand: and it is so that you carve two wooden posts in the ground near the head of the sick person at the end of both feet, whose length is that of one arm: and near his head at the end of both feet, two wooden posts.
And the length between each post shall be a palm: and now a hole has been made everywhere where the lathe runs: and all the posts are fastened firmly to the ground, so that they do not move at all.
And a round stick is inserted: and it is a purse where the ligament revolves in two openings of two sticks.
And at its end there is a hole where the stick is secured, whose length is a palm with what revolves: and the same is in two other sticks as well.
Then tie the ligaments which you tied in the chest of the sick man to the purse which is at its head: and tie the ligaments which you tied in his legs to the purse which is at his feet.
Then let a servant stand by every purse, who will twist his hand with a linen cloth, with which the purse revolves: and the physician ties Spondilem, as we have said.
This is the shape of the purse, which follows.
Then when Spondile returns and the place is tied, then it is necessary to place an adhesive plaster with egg white. Then wax. Then place a star-shaped piece of wood on the adhesive plaster, whose width is three fingers, or approximately so. And its length should be sufficient to cover the hollow of the hernia and the healthy portion of the testicle.
Then it is tied with the ligament that we have said. And the sick man should be given a subtle diet until he recovers.
If something remains as a protrusion in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and soothe, with the use of the tablets which I have mentioned for a long time. And now a sheet of lead is given there.
Sometimes a protrusion occurs at the end of the hernia sac on the back: and it is thought to be a displacement, and that bone now protrudes. It is not appropriate for this medicine to be presumed, for perhaps it will cause death.
On the Treatment of a Dislocated Ankle.
Know that the joints of the ankle and shoulder do not occur except due to dislocation; neither does it happen to them what happens to the other joints due to slight removal and hollowing out.
Indeed, the joint of the ankle is dislocated in four parts. It means that it is dislocated towards the front and the back.
And indeed it is mostly dislocated towards the inside; it is seldom dislocated towards the front or the back.
The treatment for dislocation towards the inside is: because when you join a healthy leg with an unhealthy one, it is longer; and the knee of the unhealthy one protrudes more than a healthy one, and the unhealthy one cannot double its foot near the groin; there is a place following the groin that is depressed by a depression tool because the head of the hip has already reached there.
The sign for dislocation towards the outside is that its symptoms are opposite to these symptoms.
The sign for dislocation towards the front is that it extends the healthy leg by a bend; however, it does not double it without pain in the knee; if it tries to walk, it cannot do so over it on the front side; and it retains urine, and the groin is pressed, and while walking it strikes against the heel.
The sign for dislocation towards the back is that it does not extend the knee nor can it double it before doubling the groin; and the leg is even shorter than the other one; and the groin is softened, and the head of the hip is at its place.
The reduction of this dislocation, however, is to consider at that time if the dislocation is old, which has already become chronic with the one having it. Then if you administer the reduction of it and it remains according to its position; then there is no treatment at all in it. Therefore, it should not be a presumption for him completely.
In the case where dislocation is recent, and it belongs to one of four types of dislocations: then proceed to this, so that you wish to join [the parts], and stretch it towards the inside and outside, and move it right and left: perhaps it returns, and does not need other care.
If it does not return in this manner, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong helper, and he extends the leg from its lower part: either with his hands, or with a bandage that binds above the leg over the knee: and another helper extends the hand above, so that he introduces his hands under the fingers. Then bind it with a soft bandage on the root of the hip, and hold the end of the other ligament.
And the extension is either from the front part of the groin to the neck part, or from the back to the back part.
And the extension of all [limbs] is one and the same, until the weak one is lifted with his body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is a common type among the four types.
If then the dislocation returns as we said, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary for all cases what I will say about treatment.
In the reduction of [the limb] itself, when dislocation is towards the inside, it is necessary for the weak one to lie on his healthy side. Then place a bandage on the root of the hip, in that which is between the head of the hip and the place which is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upwards from the groin to the higher part of the body, to the neck part.
Then another strong helper takes his arms and makes a large place on the weak hip small, and extends it towards the outside with a violent extension. Now, it returns to its own place.
And this is an easier species among the remaining species of treatment, by which this member is reduced.
If it is excised over you, and does not obey you for entry with this species of treatment completely: then, it is necessary that two weak feet be tied together with a strong ligament, soft, above both heels, and above both knees: and let the length of each one be four digits' width: and let the injured leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two digits.
Then, the injured one is hung over the head of a stick, which is in the house: and let its length from the ground be two arms' width.
Then command a strong youth to brace the head of the hip: and let another hang it with the injured one: and let another youth push, bracing the hip with force, for the joining returns to its own place quickly.
In its own return, when there is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the injured one lies on a platform, according to what we said in the one having a humpback. And let a ligament be tied over its leg, the injured one's own, and over its chest.
Then two sticks are placed, one at his feet and the other at his head. Then a stick is placed in the middle of the platform, firmly fixed: over which are rolled damp cloths, so as not to harm the injured one: and let the stick be between his hips, so as not to pull him down towards the lower parts due to the extension.
Then let every servant go to his place, and the doctor sets the reduction with his hand.
If it obeys in its reversal, it will be good.
And if not, place it on the same platform and press it equally, as we said in the humpback. The sick person should lie on his healthy side.
When the dislocation is towards the front, then it should be extended quickly the leg of the sick person; and this is according to the same narrative on a bench; and place the physician's palm of his right hand upon the hip of the sick person. Then bend him with one hand, and with that bending place him extended towards the lower part at the knee.
If the dislocation is towards the back: then it is not necessary to extend the sick person downwards and lift him off the ground; rather, it is necessary to place him on a hard surface, as it is also necessary if the dislocation of his shoulder is towards the outside, according to what we said about placing him on two hard surfaces: one under his face and the ligaments extended: according to what we said recently.
It is necessary that a compression be administered with a board also at the place where the joint goes out, when reduction is completed according to what is necessary.
And the sign of its reduction will not be hidden from you: and it is as if you extend two legs of the sick person; and when you see them equal, and the sick person contracts his leg, and expands himself without straining: then you know that the limb has returned, according to what is necessary: then join the pelvis, and place an ointment, and bind it with something soft, that is, a bandage: so that the shoulder does not move to any part; and let the sick person remain quiet for three or four days.
Then loosen the ligament and the ointment, and bind the leg with the other; then if you see them equal in their state, then you know that the dislocation is already fixed: so release the sick person for walking.
And if you see something softening: then return, and apply the ointment, and bind it according to your first binding; and leave it for three days as well.
Then dissolve it and make him walk slowly on both sides for some days, until he recovers well.
On Dislocations of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated to the outer, inner, and lower, that is, the back.
And it is not dislocated to the front.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you order the weak to apply his leg to his hip. If it does not stick to the hip: then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all the ways of dislocation is that you make the weak sit straight, so that he now extends his leg if he can over it, and his back touches your face, and put his foot between your feet.
Then make the knee stick with your palms, and straighten them between the fingers over the knee. Then apply with your palm the sides of the knee with force. And another servant extends his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of restoration is that the leg sticks with the hip gently, not laboriously.
Then plaster it and make the leg stick with the hip. Then bind it with a bandage for three or four days. Then dissolve it.
And the weak should not walk for some days until he is healed.
If the reduction is refused to you, then administer strong extension with bindings whose precursor was mentioned in the treatment of ankle, until it returns.
Chapter on the Treatment of Ankle Dislocation.
The heel sometimes moves out slightly and sometimes it is dislocated completely. Dislocation of it can be either internal or external.
The sign of a dislocation is that you see the calcaneum swollen and protruding towards the part to which it is dislocated.
The treatment of its removal is easy reduction; and it is that it should be extended easily with hands, and equated until it returns.
In the treatment of it when it is dislocated by a jump, it is necessary that you make the patient sit straight; and let a strong servant hold him from the back of his middle part.
Then hold with your right hand the upper part of his foot and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot near the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then, with your left hand, twice.
Then stretch it with the left hand, and push the foot's chest with the right hand without strain.
Do that twice, as we have said.
Then push the foot's chest to the leg in the third instance, and you pull the ankle.
If it returns in one or two instances according to this method, and you see the foot equated, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on it; because it itself returns.
If the reduction of it is prevented by him when we have said; then let the patient lie on his back on the ground, and fix a pillow firmly on the ground so that he falls between his hips; and you now roll over him with a cloth, so as not to hurt the patient.
Then let a servant take his hip; afterwards, let another servant extend the leg either with hands or with a rope that binds over its neck. Then all the servants should extend against its extension; and the pillow should be standing between the hips of the patient, holding him so that his body is not pulled down towards the lower part during the extension.
Then the physician reduces dislocation with his hands, and another servant holds the healthy leg downward. The dislocation returns quickly.
Whenever the dislocation returns and it is made clear to you that the reduction is good: then place plaster and wool, and tighten it firmly with bandages, and bind the foot with a bandage to the leg.
And it is necessary that you be careful or fear of the nerves which are above the calcaneum posteriorly, so that the bandage is not too tight and injures them.
Then release him for two or three days. If the bandage softens, tighten it; then release him on the third or fourth day; and forbid the weak from walking for forty days. But if he attempts to walk before that period, there is no safety unless the dislocation is loosened again and damaged, and will not accept treatment afterwards.
If a hot abscess happens to him: then it is necessary that you administer to his sedation that which was narrated before in another place about treatment, and an embrocation until it is removed.
Chapter on dislocations of the fingers of the foot and treatment.
It is necessary that it is reduced which is dislocated from them with slight extension without effort; and this certainly is not difficult, but rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in some knuckles of the foot: then it is necessary that the weak sits his foot on a level place with the ground or on a table; and he stands like walking.
Then you stand, and put your foot over that which protrudes from those joints. Then press it with your foot with force until it returns, and you see it already reduced, and the protrusion does not appear in that place.
Then place under his abdomen a table that can hold his entire foot, which has two heads. Then tie it with a fitting stitch for three days. Then loosen it and let him rest from walking for many days until it is tied and secured against reduction.
Chapter on types of dislocations with wound or fracture.
When something like this occurs, and you attempt its cure and restoration, death often follows many times; and on account of this, one should not presume to undertake such a cure except the one who is wise in the art, compassionate, pious, patient, not hasty, nor presumptuous: and that he uses at the beginning only medicines that soothe abscesses with warm things: and that he leaves the sick to divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for salvation from danger with mildness of illness, and something appears to be hopeful. Therefore attempt the reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If therefore the limb returns as we desire, then administer a regimen that soothes abscesses; and care for the wound with what is suitable for it from dry ointments.
If the dislocation is with a fracture and splinters are separated from the bone, then attempt their removal; and do what I said for simple illnesses in those places where they occurred.
And be cautious according to your ability, and keep yourself from entering the path of fear; as my previous advice warned you.
For it is better for you before your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and more healthy for your body before them at your time.
